Sarih and Kinaya in Haqiqa and Majaz
Defining sarih and kinaya as subtypes of haqiqa and majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any haqiqa or majaz as sarih or kinaya based on the clarity of the intended meaning, and differentiate the usuli definition of kinaya from the balaghi definition.

Core Idea
Sarih is clear; kinaya is hidden — but both can be literal or metaphorical in Hanafi usul.

Having understood that haqiqa and majaz can shift, we now examine their internal divisions: sarih (explicit) and kinaya (oblique). This is crucial for determining whether a text requires interpretation or can be taken at face value. The passage also contrasts the usul definition of kinaya with that of balagha.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definitions of haqiqa and majaz
Ensure you have the foundation: haqiqa is literal usage, majaz is non-literal. Both can be divided into sarih and kinaya.
2
Define sarih and kinaya as applied to haqiqa
Sarih haqiqa: the intended meaning is clear and apparent; it is still in common use. Kinaya haqiqa: the haqiqa that is abandoned in usage and its majazi meaning has become dominant; the literal meaning is hidden.
The passage says: 'That haqiqa which is not abandoned but used, that is sarih; and that haqiqa which is abandoned and its majazi meaning is predominant, that haqiqa is called kinaya.'
3
Define sarih and kinaya as applied to majaz
Sarih majaz: the metaphorical meaning is prevalent and clear. Kinaya majaz: the metaphorical meaning is rare and thus hidden.
Thus four categories: haqiqa sarih, haqiqa kinaya, majaz sarih, majaz kinaya.
4
Understand the usuli definition of kinaya
In usul, kinaya is a word used in its original haqiqi meaning, but the speaker intends a necessary concomitant (lazim) of that meaning. Example: 'tawil al-nijad' (long of peg) literally describes a long tent peg, but intends a person who is tall, because tallness is a necessary concomitant of a long peg.
This is different from majaz, where the word is used for something other than its original meaning and the original meaning is not intended.
5
Distinguish from balaghi definition
In balagha, kinaya is defined as a word from which a secondary meaning is intended while the original meaning is not intended. In usul, the original meaning is intended, but a secondary meaning is also intended via the relation of necessary concomitance.
The passage explicitly marks this distinction: 'And this kinaya is not contrary to intending the original meaning. Rather, it is used in its original meaning, but the intended [secondary] meaning is the necessary concomitant.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

صریح اور دوسرا کنا یہ ۔ تقسیم اور تعریف کنایتہ عند علماء الاصول ہے ۔ وعند علماء البیان اور علمام بیان و معانی کے نزدیک کنا یہ وہ لفظ ہے جس کے معنی موضوع لہ سے معنی ثانی مراد ہو۔ جو معنی اول کا ملزوم ہے جو ملزوم ہے ۔ کمافی طویل النجاد یہ اپنے معنی موضوت کہ میں مستعمل ہے ۔ لیکن غرض اور مقصود اسی سے طویل القامہ ہے کیونکہ طول قامتہ ملزوم ہے ۔ برائے طول النجار تو یہ اطلاق لازم اور اراده ملزوم ہوا بخلاف المجانی بخلاف مجاز کے کہ وہ بخیر ما وضع لہ میں مستعمل ہوتا ہے ۔ اور یہ ارادہ موضوع لہ کے منافی ہوتا ہے۔ یعنی مجاز میں موضوع لہ کا ارادہ نہیں ہو سکتا ۔ پھر یہ حقیقتہ و مجاز یا تو مفرد میں ہوں گے جن کی تعریفات گزر چکی ہیں ۔ دامانی الجملہ اور یا حقیقتہ و مجاز جملہ میں ہوں گے ۔
But it will be only on [horse?]. And similarly, the shar'i word salat, its application shar'an to every du'a is not correct, but shar'an its application is only to specific pillars. And it is also established that when the usage of haqiqa becomes little, it becomes majaz. And when the usage of majaz becomes abundant, it becomes haqiqa. For example, the word salat was originally haqiqa in du'a and majaz in specific pillars; now in shar'i terminology its usage in specific pillars has become abundant so that its meaning of du'a is forgotten. Therefore, in specific pillars it is haqiqa and in du'a it is majaz. Then each of haqiqa and majaz, if in itself the intended meaning is not hidden but clear, then it is sarih. And if its intended meaning is hidden, then it is kinaya. So the haqiqa that is not abandoned but used, that is sarih because its meaning is apparent. And that haqiqa which is abandoned in usage and its majazi meaning is predominant, that haqiqa is called kinaya. And majaz with predominant usage becomes sarih, and majaz that is not predominant becomes kinaya. Know that sarih and kinaya are two types of haqiqa; these two are with respect to the haqiqi meaning. And sarih and kinaya that are two types of majaz; they are sarih and kinaya with respect to the majazi meaning. Thus haqiqa also became two types: sarih and kinaya, and majaz also became two types: one sarih and the other kinaya. The division and definition of kinaya according to usul scholars is such. And according to scholars of bayan, kinaya is a word from which a secondary meaning is intended, which is the necessary concomitant (lazim) of the original meaning. That is, applying the necessary and intending the necessary concomitant is called kinaya. And this kinaya is not contrary to intending the original meaning. Rather, it is used in its original meaning, but the intended [secondary] meaning is the necessary concomitant. For example, 'tawil al-nijad' is used in its original meaning, but the purpose is 'tall in stature' because tallness is the necessary concomitant of long peg. Thus it is applying the necessary and intending the necessary concomitant, unlike majaz which is used in something other than what it is placed for and intending the original meaning is contrary to it. That is, in majaz, the original meaning cannot be intended. Then this haqiqa and majaz are either in individual words (mufrad) whose definitions have passed, or in sentences (jumal).
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage defines and elaborates on the concepts of sarih and kinaya within haqiqa and majaz. It begins by illustrating how a shift in frequency of usage transforms haqiqa into majaz and vice versa, using the example of the word salat: originally haqiqa for supplication and majaz for the formal prayer, but due to predominant shar'i usage, the roles reversed. Then it explains that both haqiqa and majaz can be either sarih (clear and apparent) or kinaya (hidden and requiring inference). The key criterion is whether the intended meaning is evident or concealed. A haqiqa that is still in common use is sarih; a haqiqa that is abandoned and whose majazi meaning dominates is considered kinaya. Conversely, a majaz that is commonly used is sarih, and a rare majaz is kinaya. The passage then provides the usul scholars' definition of kinaya: a word used in its original meaning but intending a secondary meaning that is a necessary concomitant of the original, like 'tawil al-nijad' to mean a tall person. This differs from majaz, where the word is used for something other than its original meaning and the original meaning cannot be intended. Finally, it notes that haqiqa and majaz can occur in single words or complete sentences. The discussion is foundational for understanding how language functions in legal interpretation.

Key Terms
صريح (sarih): A haqiqa or majaz whose intended meaning is clear and obvious.
كناية (kinaya): In usul: a word used in its literal meaning while intending a necessary concomitant (lazim). In balagha: a word from which a secondary meaning is intended while the literal meaning is not intended but possible.
لازم (lazim): A necessary concomitant; the secondary meaning that naturally follows from the literal meaning.
غالب الاستعمال (ghalib al-isti'mal): Predominant in usage; determines whether a haqiqa or majaz is sarih or kinaya.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi usul, how is kinaya defined differently from its balaghi definition?
A) In usul, the literal meaning is not intended; in balagha, it is intended.
B) In usul, the literal meaning is intended along with a secondary meaning; in balagha, only the secondary meaning is intended.
C) In usul, kinaya is always haqiqa; in balagha, it is always majaz.
D) There is no difference.

Key Takeaway
Sarih and kinaya are subdivisions of both haqiqa and majaz, determined by clarity of meaning; the usuli kinaya is distinct from its balaghi counterpart, retaining literal intention.

Ma'na, Mafhum, and Musamma: Foundations of Haqiqa and Majaz
Explaining the relationship between lafz, ma'na, mafhum, and musamma in haqiqa/majaz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ma'na, mafhum, and musamma, and apply the haqiqa/majaz distinction to a Qur'anic text.

Core Idea
A word's application to its musamma can be haqiqa or majaz depending on whether the original meaning is present.

Usul al-fiqh relies on precise linguistic concepts. When a word is used in legal texts, we must determine whether it is applied literally (haqiqa) or metaphorically (majaz). This passage unpacks the key terms ma'na, mafhum, and musamma, and shows how temporal change can shift a word from haqiqa to majaz.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms
From the passage, extract the definitions of ma'na, mafhum, and musamma. Note the different perspectives: intention, derivation, and coining.
Ma'na is what is intended; mafhum is what is understood; musamma is the named entity.
2
Distinguish their scopes
Observe that the author says 'the word is specific to ma'na, not to individuals, while musamma encompasses both.' Understand why this distinction matters for haqiqa/majaz.
3
Analyze the haqiqa/majaz condition
When a word is applied to a musamma, if the original meaning is actually present (hassil), it is haqiqa; if absent, it is majaz. The author notes why he omitted explicit mention of haqiqa.
The focus is on types of majaz.
4
Apply to the Qur'anic example
Work through 'And give to the orphans their wealth.' At the time of revelation, the addressees were orphans in the past; at the moment of giving they may be adults. This temporal change makes the usage majaz.
The word 'orphans' no longer applies literally at the time of action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا اطلقت لفظا علی مسمئی جب آپ لفظ کا اطلاق مسمی پر کریں تو یہ مسمی پر اطلاق کرنا اس کو بھی شامل ہے کہ اطلاق لفظ علی المعنی کیا جائے اور اس کو بھی کہ اطلاق لفظ ان افراد پر کیا جائے جن پر معنی صادق آتا ہے۔ مدلول لفظ بایں حیثیت که قصد و عنى من اللفظ تو اس کو معنی کہتے ہیں ۔ اگر بائیں حیثیت ملحوظ ہو کہ حاصل من اللفظ ہے تو اسے مفہوم کہتے ہیں۔ اور اگر بائیں حیثیت رکھیں کہ اس کے لئے ایک اسم وضع کیا گیا ہے تو اسے مسمی کہتے ۔ البتہ لفظ معنی مختص بنفس المفہوم سے دون الافراد اور لفظ مسمى يشملها وكان ينبغي ان يقول يعني لائق یہ تھا کہ مصنفہ یوں کہنا کہ اگر لفظ سے ارادہ عین موضوع لی ہو تو حقیقتہ ہے ۔ لیکن اس قسم کی حقیقتہ کو ذکر نہیں کیا کیونکہ مقصود اصلی انواع مجازات کا ذکر کرتا ہے اس لئے کہا واردت یعنی جب آپ لفظ کا اطلاق علی المسمی کریں اور ارادہ غیر موضوع لہ کا ہو تو پھر فالمعنی الحقیقی ان حصل له اگر معنی حقیقی ہیں مسمی کو بالفعل حاصل ہے۔ بعض از مان میں اگر یہ معنی حقیقی اس کو ماضی میں حاصل تھا جیسے وآتوا الیتامی اموالہم تو یہ اشخاص ماضی میں یتامی تھے اور جب ان کے حوالہ مال کیا جا رہا ہے اس وقت تو بالغ ہو کر سیمی سے خارج
And when you apply a word to a musamma (named entity), this application to the musamma includes both application of the word to the meaning (ma'na) and application to the individuals to which the meaning applies. The signification (madlul) of the word, from the perspective that it is intended from the word, is called ma'na. If considered from the perspective that it is derived from the word, it is called mafhum. And if considered from the perspective that a name is coined for it, it is called musamma. However, the word is specific to the meaning (ma'na) itself, not to the individuals, while the word musamma encompasses them. And it was fitting that the author should say: 'If you intend the exact original meaning, it is haqiqa.' But he did not mention this type of haqiqa because the main purpose is to mention the types of majaz. Therefore he said: 'I intended (aradtu) that when you apply a word to the musamma and intend other than the original meaning, then the real meaning (al-ma'na al-haqiqi) if it is actually present in the musamma... In some cases, if this real meaning was present in the past, like 'And give to the orphans their wealth' – these persons were orphans in the past, but at the time of handing over wealth, they have become adults and left the category [of orphans].
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage clarifies the relationship between words and what they signify. When a word is applied to a musamma (the named thing), it can refer either to the conceptual meaning (ma'na) or to the external individuals that instantiate that meaning. The term ma'na is used when focusing on the intention behind the word; mafhum is the understanding derived from the word; and musamma is the entity for which the word is coined. The author notes that the word (lafz) is specifically tied to the ma'na, not to the individuals, whereas musamma can encompass both. The author then explains why the writer did not explicitly mention that applying a word to its original meaning is haqiqa: because the focus is on listing the types of majaz. So when the author says 'when you apply the word to the musamma and intend other than the original meaning', then the discussion of haqiqa arises only if the real meaning is present. The example from the Qur'an 'And give to the orphans their wealth' is analyzed: the word 'orphans' originally means those who lost their father before puberty. At the time of giving wealth, they may have become adults, so the term is applied in a metaphorical sense (majaz) because the original meaning is no longer literally true at the moment of application. This illustrates how temporal changes can affect the haqiqa/majaz classification of words in legal texts.

Key Terms
معنى (ma'na): The meaning intended from the word (qasadat wa 'uniya min al-lafz).
مفهوم (mafhum): The understood meaning derived from the word (alhasil min al-lafz).
مسمى (musamma): The entity for which the word is coined; it can include both the meaning and its individuals.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the analysis of the Qur'anic phrase 'And give to the orphans their wealth' (وَآتُوا الْيَتَامَىٰ أَمْوَالَهُمْ), why is the word 'orphans' considered majaz in this context according to the passage?
A) Because orphans never become adults
B) Because at the time of giving, the recipients may have grown up and are no longer orphans literally
C) Because the word 'orphan' is never used literally in the Qur'an
D) Because the wealth is given to them in the past

Key Takeaway
A word applied to a musamma is haqiqa if the original meaning is present at the time of application; otherwise it is majaz, as illustrated by the 'orphans' verse.

The Nine Types of Metaphorical Relations in Hanafi Usul
Definition and enumeration of metaphorical relations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the nine علاقات مجاز and apply the condition of descriptive relation to distinguish valid metaphor from invalid extension.

Core Idea
A metaphor is valid only when a clear, evident relation ties the literal to the intended meaning.

This passage introduces the foundational categories of metaphorical relations (علاقات مجاز) in Hanafi usul. Understanding these types is crucial for correctly interpreting legal texts where words are used non-literally. The author highlights two specific relations—conditionality and descriptiveness—and lists the full set of nine that scholars rely upon.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two علاقات explicitly described
From the Arabic text, locate the discussion of العلاقة الشرطية (conditional relation) and العلاقة الوصفية (descriptive relation). Note how each is defined and exemplified.
The conditional relation applies when the term denotes a condition for the thing signified; the descriptive relation relies on a shared attribute.
2
Understand the condition for descriptive relation
The text specifies that the descriptive relation requires the attribute to be 'بین اور واضح' (clear and evident). Consider why this condition is necessary to prevent arbitrary metaphorical extensions.
Compare with the example of 'asad' for a courageous man: courage is clearly associated with lions.
3
Recognize the full list of nine
The author states that the relations of metaphor are nine in total. From the text, extract as many as possible (الاستعداد، القابلة، الجزئية، الحلول، السببية، الشرطية، الوصفية, and two more that may be unclear due to OCR).
The first item 'الکون' may be 'الكون' (existence) or a copyist error. In standard Hanafi works, the nine are well-known.
4
Apply to a legal example
Take a Quranic verse like 'فَاقْتُلُوا أَنفُسَكُمْ' (kill yourselves, 2:54). The word 'anfusakum' is used metaphorically for 'brothers' (since killing oneself is impossible). Identify which علاقة justifies this usage—likely الجزئية (part-whole) because the individual is part of the community.
This shows how علاقات are used in tafsir and fiqh to derive correct meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرط ہے تو علم مشروط ہوا بشرط تحق المعلوم تو یہ اطلاق علم على المعلوم اطلاق مشروط على الشرط ہوا۔ اسے علاقہ شرطیہ کہا جاتا ہے۔ اديكون صفة يا وہ لازم صفتہ برائے ملزوم ہوگا - وهو الاستعارة سیا ستعارہ کہلاتا ہے لیکن اسکے اندر شرط ہے کہ یہ وصف بین اور واضح ہو ، کالاسد جیسے اسد کا اطلاق کریں اور اس سے مراد اس کا لازم ہو یعنی شجاعت تو یہ لازم وصف بین ہے اسد کی تو اطلاق اسد علی زید بائیں اعتبار کہ وہ شجاع ہے اسے استعارہ کہا جاتا ہے۔ جیسے ریت اسدا بر می تو اس کو علاقہ وصفیہ کہا جاتا ہے. تو مصنف کے نزدیک علاقات مجاز کل تسعہ ہوئے ۔ الکون الاول. الاستعداد القابلة - الجزئية - الحلول - البية - الشرطية . الوصفية .
If there is a condition, then knowledge became conditioned upon the realization of the known, so this application of knowledge to the known is the application of the conditioned to the condition. This is called the conditional relationship. Or it becomes a description, that is, a necessary attribute for what is described - and that is metaphor, but it has a condition that this description be clear and evident, like 'asad' (lion): you apply 'asad' and intend its necessary attribute, i.e., courage, which is a clear necessary attribute of lion. So applying 'asad' to Zayd on the basis that he is courageous is called metaphor. Like 'ra'aytu asadan' (I saw a lion) - this is called the descriptive relationship. According to the author, the relationships of metaphor are nine in total: first [?], preparedness, receptivity, part-whole, indwelling, causality, conditionality, descriptiveness.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage explains the types of علاقات (relationships) that justify metaphorical usage (مجاز) in language. The author begins by discussing the conditional relationship (علاقہ شرطیہ): when a term is applied to something that is a condition for its meaning, like calling knowledge a condition for the known. Then the descriptive relationship (علاقہ وصفیہ): applying the name of something to another based on a clear attribute, such as calling a courageous man 'lion' due to the attribute of courage. The author then states that there are nine such relationships according to the commentator (مصنف). The list includes: preparedness (الاستعداد), receptivity (القابلة), part-whole (الجزئية), indwelling (الحلول), causality (السببية), conditionality (الشرطية), and descriptiveness (الوصفية). Note: The first item 'الکون' is ambiguous in the OCR; it likely refers to another علاقة, possibly 'الكون' (existence) or a typo for 'السببية' which appears later. The Hanafi tradition categorizes these علاقات as the basis for metaphorical extension in both linguistic and legal terms.

Key Terms
علاقہ شرطیہ (alāqa shartiyya): A relationship based on conditionality, where the metaphorical meaning is a condition for the literal meaning.
علاقہ وصفیہ (alāqa wasfiyya): A relationship based on a clear descriptive attribute shared between the original and metaphorical referent.
استعارہ (isti‘āra): Metaphor, using a word in a non-literal sense due to a shared attribute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for the descriptive relationship (علاقہ وصفیہ)?
A) The attribute must be obscure
B) The attribute must be clear and evident
C) The attribute must be non-existent
D) The attribute must be religious

Key Takeaway
The validity of metaphorical usage in Hanafi usul depends on a clear and recognized relation between literal and intended meanings, of which nine types are enumerated.

Causality and Part-Whole in Metaphorical Extension: Two Mutual Relations
Detailed analysis of causal and part-whole metaphor relations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of relationship (causality and part-whole) that allow bidirectional metaphor in Hanafi usul, and apply the rule that part-for-whole metaphor is only valid when the part entails the whole.

Core Idea
Metaphor flows both ways in causal and part-whole relations—but only when the part is essential for the whole.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, the permissibility of metaphorical extension depends on the nature of the relationship between the literal and intended meaning. This passage examines two such relationships: causality (علة ومعلول) and part-whole (جزء وكل). In both, under certain conditions, you can apply the name of the original term to the derived term and vice versa. Understanding these conditions is essential for accurate interpretation of legal texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two relationships
Recognize that the passage discusses two distinct relationships: causal (cause-effect) and part-whole. Metaphor can operate in both directions (from cause to effect and from effect to cause, from whole to part and from part to whole) but with different constraints.
2
Analyze the causal relationship
For causality, the passage explains that both cause and effect can be considered 'original' depending on intention. The cause is original in external existence, but the effect can be original in mental intention as the final goal. Thus, applying 'cause' to mean 'effect' (e.g., 'rain' for 'crops') and 'effect' to mean 'cause' (e.g., 'crops' for 'rain') are both valid.
Key concept: originality (اصالت) is not fixed to one side in causality.
3
Analyze the part-whole relationship
For part-whole, the default is that the whole is original, so applying 'whole' to 'part' (e.g., 'cup' for 'water') is universally valid. However, applying 'part' to 'whole' is only valid when the part is essential for the whole's existence (مستلزم للكل), such as 'neck' for a human, but not 'hand'.
The condition for part-for-whole metaphor: the part must entail the whole (i.e., the whole cannot exist without that part).
4
Apply to common examples
Test the rule: 'He freed a neck' (أعتق رقبة) is a valid metaphor for freeing an entire slave because the neck is essential. But 'He gave a hand' cannot mean the whole person because a hand is not essential.
This example appears in Hanafi fiqh regarding expiation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا عرفت ان مبنى المجاز الخ جب یہ معلوم ہوا کہ معنی مجاز ۔ اس پر ہے کہ اسم ملزوم کا اطلاق على اللازم کیا جاتا ہے۔ یعنی اطلاق ملزوم اور ارادہ لازم ہوتا ہے ۔ واللزوم اصل واللازم الم توجب اصلیتہ و فرعیته ورامین سے ہو سکے تو مجاز طرفین سے جاری ہوگا۔ کا لعلة مع المعلول . جیسے علتہ و معلول اور معلول ایسی شئی ہے ۔ جو علہ غائیہ برائے علتہ فاصلہ ہوتی ہے ۔ اگر چہ علہ اصل کا درجہ رکھتی ہے کیونکہ محلول اس کی طرف محتاج ہوتا ہے لیکن وہ معلول جو مقصود بالذات ہو تو وہ بمنزلہ علیہ غائیہ کے ہو کر اصل قرار پاتا ہے ۔ غایتہ اگرچہ وجود خارجی کے لحاظ سے فاعل کی معلول ہے اور متاخر فی الوجود لیکن غایتہ باعتبار وجود ذہنی یعنی جو اس کا وجود فی ذھن الفاعل متصور ہے۔ دہ فاعلیتہ فاعل کے لئے علقہ باعثہ ہے اور اس وجود میں فاصلیہ فاعل سے مقدم ہے ۔ تو ایسی علتہ و معلول کے اندر اصلیتہ و فرعیتہ جانبین سے قائم ہو سکتی ہے۔ اور مجاز طرفین سے جاری ہو سکتا ہے ۔ اطلاق علقہ ارادہ معلول اور با عکس بالعکس دونوں صحیح ہو سکتے ہیں ۔ وكا لجزء مع الكل فان الجزء الخ یعنی جزء تابع للکل ہے۔ یعنی وہ لفظ جو موضوع للکل ہے ۔ جز اس کی نسبت تابع ہے ۔ خان الجزء اس لئے کہ جز اسی لفظ سے تبعیۃ الکل مفہوم ہوتی ہے ۔ یعنی جب اس لفظ موضوع للکل سے کل مفہوم ہو گا تو اس کل کے ضمن میں تبعاً جز بھی مفہوم ہوگی تو اس لحاظ سے کل اصل ہوا ۔ فیصح ان يطلق میں صحیح ہے کہ یہ لفظ موضوع للکل کا اطلاق کر کے ارادہ جزیرہ موضوع لہ کا کیا جائے ۔ والکل محتاج الى المجن اور کل اپنے وجود میں محتاج الى الجزر ہوتا ہے ۔ کیونکہ وجود کل بدون وجود الاجز آ نہیں ہو سکتا تو فيكون الجز اصلا تو اس لحاظ سے جز بدرجہ اصل ہوئی۔ فیصح ان پرار الکل الخ تو یہ صحیح ہوگا کہ اطلاق اس لفظ کا کیا جائے جو موضوع للجز ہے اور ارادہ کل کا کیا جائے تو یہ مجاز طرفین سے جاری ہوا ۔ فاطلاق اسم الکل اخر یعنی اسم کل کا اطلاق علی المجز کرنا مطرد اور کلی ہے۔ وعكسه غير مطرد يعني اطلاق اسم الجزء علی الکل مطرد اور کلی نہیں ہے یعنی ہر جز سے ارادہ کل کرنا صحیح نہیں ہے۔ بل يجوزنى صور بلکہ ایسی صورت میں جائز ہوگا۔ جہاں جز مستلزم للکل ہوگی کا رقبة والراس جیسے مثلا رقبہ و
And when it is known that the basis of metaphor is that the name of the necessary (ملزوم) is applied to the contingent (لازم) – that is, applying the necessary and intending the contingent – and necessity is original, and the contingent is such that its originality and subordination can be from both sides, then metaphor can flow from both sides. Like cause with effect: cause and effect, and effect is something that is a final cause for the efficient cause. Although cause holds the rank of origin because the effect needs it, but if the effect is intended in itself, then it becomes like a final cause and is considered original. The final cause, although in external existence is an effect of the agent and posterior in existence, but in mental existence – which is its existence in the mind of the agent – it is a motivating cause for the agency and precedes the agent in this existence. So in such cause and effect, originality and subordination can be established from both sides, and metaphor can flow from both sides: applying the cause and intending the effect, and vice versa, both are valid. And like the part with the whole: the part is subordinate to the whole. That is, the word that is placed for the whole, the part is subordinate to it. Because the part is understood from that word through the subordination of the whole. That is, when the word placed for the whole is understood as the whole, then within that whole the part is also understood by subordination. In this respect, the whole is original. So it is valid to apply the word placed for the whole and intend the part. And the whole needs the parts, because the existence of the whole without the parts is impossible. So in this respect the part becomes original. So it is valid to apply the word placed for the part and intend the whole. Thus metaphor flows from both sides. So applying the name of the whole to the part is universal and general. But its opposite, i.e., applying the name of the part to the whole, is not universal and general. That is, it is not valid to intend the whole from every part. Rather it is valid in cases where the part entails the whole, like 'neck' (رقبة) and 'head' (راس). For example, 'رقبة' and 'راس'.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage elaborates two key علاقات: causality (علة ومعلول) and part-whole (جزء وكل). In causality, metaphor can run both ways: applying 'cause' to mean 'effect' and vice versa, depending on the context and intention. For instance, 'rain' (cause) may be used for 'crops' (effect), and 'crops' for 'rain' if the crops are the ultimate goal. The author explains that while cause is nominally original, the effect can become original in mental intention. For part-whole, the relationship is generally that the whole is original, but the part may be considered original when the whole depends on it. Applying 'whole' to 'part' is always valid (e.g., 'he drank the cup' meaning the water), but applying 'part' to 'whole' is only valid when the part is essential for the whole's existence, like 'neck' or 'head' for a human. You cannot apply 'hand' to mean the entire person because a person can exist without a hand. This illustrates the Hanafi principle of contextual constraint in metaphorical usage.

Key Terms
ملزوم (malzūm): The necessary term, the one whose implication is necessary.
لازم (lāzim): The necessary concomitant, the implied meaning.
مطرد (mutṭarid): Universal or generally applicable; here, a metaphorical extension that applies in all cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of a valid part-to-whole metaphor?
A) Using 'hand' to mean the entire person
B) Using 'cup' to mean the water in it
C) Using 'neck' to mean the entire slave
D) Using 'rain' to mean the clouds

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, bidirectional metaphor is valid in causal relations and part-whole relations, but part-to-whole is restricted to essential parts that entail the whole.

Ilaqah Sababiyyah: Causal Relation as a Valid Ground for Majaz
Explaining 'ilaqah sababiyyah and its application to nikah with lafz hibah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the meaning of 'ilaqah sababiyyah, distinguish it from 'ilaqah mushabahah, and apply it to derive the validity of using causal words in legal contracts as majaz.

Core Idea
Causality ('ilaqah sababiyyah) is a valid relation for majaz: using the word for the cause to mean the effect.

In Hanafi usul, majaz (metaphorical use) is only valid when there is a clear connection ('ilaqah) between the literal and intended meanings. The author introduces another type of 'ilaqah besides similarity (mushabahah) — the causal relation ('ilaqah sababiyyah). This lesson examines this usul principle and its application to the example of marriage contracted with the word 'hibah' (gift), where the word for ownership of corpus is used to mean ownership of usufruct.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of 'ilaqah
The author contrasts 'ilaqah sababiyyah with the previously discussed 'ilaqah mushabahah. Find the phrase where he mentions that 'ilaqah sababiyyah is also effective for majaz in legal matters.
Look for: 'اور شرعیات میں مجازہ کیلئے علاقہ سبیہ بھی کارآمد ہے'.
2
Analyze the example of the Prophet's marriage
The example: the Prophet's marriage with the word hibah. Understand why hibah, literally meaning transfer of ownership of corpus (milk al-raqabah), can validly be used to mean marriage (milk al-mut'ah) through causal relation.
The relation: milk al-raqabah is the cause of milk al-mut'ah (as with a slave-girl).
3
Extend the principle to others
The Hanafi position extends this validity to all individuals, not just the Prophet. Note the condition that the woman must be free; for a slave, hibah establishes ownership of corpus itself.
Text says: 'وكذا نکاح غيره عندنا'.
4
Locate the Hanafi usul principle behind this
The principle: when a word is placed for a cause, it can be used metaphorically for its effect due to the causal connection. This is a valid form of majaz recognized by Hanafi usul.
Key point for istinbat (derivation).

النبی اور بعد وفات النبی انھیں کے لئے خالص مخلص ہیں۔ لاني اللفظ یعنی خلوص و اختصاص لفظ میں نہیں۔ کیونکہ مجاز حضرت رسالہ علیہ السلام کے ساتھ مختص ہیں ہے کیونکہ یہ تو ایک لسانی اور لفظی مسئلہ ہے ۔ اس کا کسی کے ساتھ اختصاص نہیں ہوسکتا۔ وايضا تلك الامور الجنسية امام شافعی کی دوسری دلیل که مشروعیتہ نکاح مصالح کے لئے ہے۔ یہ اس کا جواب ہے . حاصل جواب یہ کہ نکاح کے مشروعیتہ اور وضع ان مصالح کے لئے نہیں بلکہ یہ نکاح کے فروع و ثمرات ہیں۔ اور نکاح کی بنا اور وضع اس کے لئے ہے کہ خاوند کو منکوحہ پر ملک حاصل ہو جائے ۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ خاوند کے ذمہ بعوض ملک نکاح مہر لازم ہو جاتی ہے اور طلاق بھی خاوند کے قبضہ میں ہے ۔ تو یہ صاف دلیل ہے کہ وہ مالک ہے اور عقد نکاح برائے اثبات ملک ہے۔ لوکان وضعہ اگر وضع نکاح ان مصالح کی خاطر ہوتی تو یہ تمام مصالح مشترک بین الزوجین ہیں تو پھر مهر بحق زوجہ واجب على الزوج نہ ہوتی اور ملک طلاق صرف زوج کے ساتھ مخصوص نہ ہوتا ۔ فاذا كان الخ تو جب مہر زوج پر واجب ہے اور طلاق بھی اس کے ملک و قبضہ میں ہے تو معلوم ہوا کہ نکاح کی وضع ملک الزوج علی الزوجتہ کے لئے ہے ۔ وانا صح بلفظین جب نکاح ان دو لفظوں کے ساتھ صحیح ہے جو لغتہ وال علی الملک نہیں ہیں یعنی لفظ نکاح و تزویج فاولى ان يصح تو پھر ان الفاظ کے ساتھ جو دال علی الملک ہیں جیسے لفظ بہ وغیرہ ان کے ساتھ انعقاد نکاح بطریق اولی صحیح ہونا چاہتے . وانما يصح بهما أى يصح النكاح بلفظ النكاح والتزويج لإنهما صارا علمين لهذا العقد الى عقد النكاح جواب اشکال یعنی وانما یصح الخریہ ایک اشکال کا جواب ہے ۔ اشکال یہ کہ جب لفظ نكاح وتزويج لخته دال علی الملک نہیں ہیں تو پھر ان لفظوں کے ساتھ نکاح صحیح نہ ہونا چاہئے کیونکہ ان کی ملک متد پر نہ دلالت ہے نہ وضع تو اس کا جواب دیا کہ یہ دونوں لفظ یعنی لفظ نکاح اور تزویج اس عقد شرعی کے لئے بمنزلہ علم کے ہو چکے ہیں یعنی جیسے علم علی التعیین مسمی کے لئے موضوع ہوتا ہے۔ اسی طرح شرعا یہ الفاظ اس عقد کے لئے موضوع ہو چکے ہیں اور اعلام میں معنی لغوی کی رعایتہ لازمی نہیں ہے ۔ وکذ ا ینعقد النكاح اس
There are three conditions. And the clear necessary element for legal acts: the necessary element of legal acts is both textual and meaningful, which is outside their meaning. However, it applies to legal acts that from the conception of legal acts, this meaning necessarily comes to mind. This is the same meaning of legitimacy. And 'sababiyyah' (causality) is an 'atf' (conjunction) on his statement 'ittisal al-j'? Its 'atf' is 'ittisal' on 'fi ma'na al-mashru'iyyah'. And 'ittisal fi ma'na al-mashru'iyyah' was an 'ilaqah mushabahah' (relation of similarity), and in legal matters for majaz, the relation of similarity is also effective. 'Ka nikahihi 'alayhi al-salam' etc. This is an example of 'ilaqah sababiyyah'. That is, the marriage of the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, is contracted with the word hibah: when a free woman says, 'I gift myself to you, O Messenger of Allah,' and the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, accepts it, then the marriage is contracted: 'Indeed, she offered herself to the Prophet.' So the word hibah is placed in reality for ownership of the corpus, and the placement of marriage is for ownership of usufruct. That is, ownership of the corpus is the cause of ownership of usufruct. For example, when a slave-girl comes into the ownership of a person, ownership of usufruct also accrues to him, and intercourse with an owned slave-girl is legally permissible. So if the word that was placed for ownership of the corpus is applied and ownership of usufruct is intended, then with the relation of causality this majaz is valid. Likewise, the marriage of others according to us: According to us, the marriage of persons other than the Prophet, peace be upon him, is also contracted with the word hibah with the relation of causality, if the woman is free. Because if the woman is a slave-girl and her owner says 'I gift her to you', then according to us 'asabah' is established and ownership of the corpus is obtained. If the woman is free and she or her guardian says 'I gift myself to you' or 'I gift my ward to you' and the addressee accepts, then the marriage is contracted. According to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, the marriage of someone other than the Prophet, peace be upon him, is not contracted by the word hibah, but only by the word nikah or tazwij, because Allah Most High said 'Khalisatan laka' (pure for you), so it is understood that the conclusion of marriage with the word hibah is specific to the Prophet, peace be upon him. And also because it is a legal contract: Imam Shafi'i's second proof is that the marriage contract is a contract whose legitimacy is for innumerable interests, such as establishing lineage, continuity of the human race, abstinence from adultery/fornication, attaining ihsan, creating affection between the two, mutual assistance in livelihood, and many other benefits too numerous to count. Other than the two words (nikah and tazwij) are incapable of indicating these interests, so marriage is contracted only with these two words. Other words are incapable of indication, so marriage is not contracted with them. We say: 'Al-makhlas fi al-hukm' – this is the response to the first proof of 'Khalisatan laka', i.e., the ruling of hibah is specific to the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, meaning marriage without dower is contracted... The verse means that the validity of marriage with the word hibah without obligation of dower is specific to you. As for persons other than the Prophet, peace be upon him, their marriage with the word hibah is contracted, but dower becomes obligatory. Also, it is possible: this is the second response to 'Khalisatan laka'. The meaning of the verse can also be that 'Khalisatan laka' is related to 'our giving you your wives' and grammatically it is a hal (circumstantial accusative) from 'azwajik'. The meaning would be: 'We have given you your wives, while they are pure for you', i.e., the wives of the Prophet are specific to him and it is not permissible for anyone else to marry them, as Allah said about them: 'and his wives are their mothers', whether the Prophet divorces them during his lifetime (God forbid) or after his death. So these pure wives are purely exclusive to him during the Prophet's life and after his death. Because exclusivity is not in the word. Because majaz is specific to the Prophet? Rather, this is a linguistic and verbal issue; it cannot be specific to anyone. And also those generic matters: this is a response to Imam Shafi'i's second proof that the legitimacy of marriage is for interests. The essence of the response: The legitimacy and placement of marriage is not for these interests; rather, these are branches and fruits of marriage. The foundation and placement of marriage is for the husband to acquire ownership over the wife. This is why the husband is obligated to pay dower in exchange for ownership in marriage, and divorce is in the husband's control. This is clear evidence that he is owner and the marriage contract is for establishing ownership. If its placement were for these interests, then all these interests are common between the spouses; then dower would not be obligatory on the husband in favor of the wife, and the right of divorce would not be specific to the husband. So since dower is obligatory on the husband and divorce is in his ownership and control, it is understood that the placement of marriage is for the husband's ownership over the wife. And since marriage is valid with two words that are not indicative of ownership in language, i.e., the words nikah and tazwij, then it is more appropriate that it should be valid with words that indicate ownership, like the word hibah, etc. It is valid with them: the marriage is valid with the words nikah and tazwij because they have become technical terms for this contract. This is a response to an objection: the objection being that if the words nikah and tazwij are not indicative of ownership, then marriage should not be valid with them because they do not indicate ownership of usufruct either in language or placement. The response: these two words have become like proper nouns for this legal contract; just as a proper noun is placed for a specific named entity, similarly, in Shariah, these words have been placed for this contract, and in proper nouns, consideration of the literal meaning is not necessary. And similarly, marriage is contracted...
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul discussion on the types of 'ilaqat (relations) that make majaz valid, specifically 'ilaqah sababiyyah (causal relation), and its application to the ruling of contracting marriage with the word hibah (gift). The author begins by explaining that for legal acts, there is a necessary meaning that comes to mind; this meaning is the meaning of legitimacy. Then he discusses the conjunction of 'ilaqah sababiyyah, noting that previously they discussed 'ilaqah mushabahah (similarity), but here the focus is on 'ilaqah sababiyyah, which is also effective for majaz in legal matters. He gives the example of the Prophet's marriage: when a woman gifted herself to the Prophet using the word 'hibah', the marriage was valid. The reason is that hibah literally means transfer of ownership of the corpus (milk al-raqabah), but marriage involves ownership of usufruct (milk al-mut'ah). Since ownership of the corpus is a cause for ownership of usufruct (as in the case of a slave-girl where owning her allows intercourse), using the word for the cause (hibah) to mean the effect (marriage) is a valid majaz with causal relation. The author then extends this to others: according to the Hanafi school, the marriage of anyone other than the Prophet is also valid with the word hibah, provided the woman is free. If the woman is a slave, her master saying 'I gift her to you' establishes ownership, which is not marriage but a transfer of slaves. For a free woman, her offer of 'hibah' and the man's acceptance constitutes a valid marriage contract, with the obligation of mahr (dower). Then the author presents the Shafi'i position: Imam al-Shafi'i holds that marriage with the word hibah is only for the Prophet, based on the Quranic verse 'Khalisatan laka' (pure for you) and on the nature of marriage as a contract for numerous interests that only the specific words of nikah and tazwij can fulfill. The author provides two Hanafi responses: (1) The exclusivity in the verse refers to the ruling of marriage without dower, not to the validity of using the word hibah. The Prophet's marriage with hibah is valid without mahr, but for others, mahr is obligatory. Alternatively, the verse is about the exclusivity of the Prophet's wives to him, not about the word. (2) The interests attributed to marriage (lineage, etc.) are not the primary purpose of the contract; rather, the primary purpose is to establish the husband's ownership over the wife. This is proven by the fact that dower is obligatory on the husband and divorce is in his hand. If the contract were for mutual interests, these rights would be symmetrical. Since they are asymmetrical, the contract is for ownership. Therefore, words that indicate ownership (like hibah) are even more appropriate than words that do not (like nikah and tazwij, which are technical terms). The passage ends by addressing the objection that nikah and tazwij are valid even though they do not indicate ownership; the response is that they have become technical terms for the contract, so their literal meaning is not required.

Key Terms
علاقہ سببیہ ('ilaqah sababiyyah): A relation where one thing is the cause of another; used for valid majaz when the word for the cause is used to mean the effect.
مجاز (majaz): Use of a word in a non-literal sense because of a connection ('ilaqah) between the literal and intended meanings.
ملک الرقبہ (milk al-raqabah): Ownership of the corpus (the thing itself), as opposed to ownership of usufruct.
ملک المتعہ (milk al-mut'ah): Ownership of usufruct (the right to benefit from something, e.g., sexual enjoyment in marriage).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what makes the word hibah (gift) valid for marriage despite being literally for ownership of corpus?
A) The word hibah has been redefined in Shariah to mean marriage.
B) Ownership of corpus is a cause for ownership of usufruct, so using the cause to mean the effect is a valid majaz.
C) The Prophet exclusively permitted it and others can follow his example.
D) The Quran explicitly states that hibah can be used for marriage.

Key Takeaway
'Ilaqah sababiyyah (causal relation) is a valid connection for majaz in Hanafi usul, allowing words placed for a cause to be used for their effect.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
12 min
The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute on Marriage Contracted with the Word Hibah
Madhhab dispute on marriage with lafz hibah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on the validity of marriage with lafz hibah, explain the Quranic proof cited by each school, and evaluate the Hanafi response using usul principles.

Core Idea
For Hanafis, nikah with hibah is valid for all; Shafi'is restrict it to the Prophet based on 'Khalisatan laka' and the nature of marriage.

The application of 'ilaqah sababiyyah to marriage with the word hibah leads to a well-known dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. The source text presents the Shafi'i objections and the Hanafi responses in detail, showcasing how usul principles such as 'amm and khass, and the purpose of the contract, are deployed in inter-madhhab debate. This lesson focuses on the structure of the dispute and the thamarah al-khilaf.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi position is that marriage is valid with the word hibah for all individuals. For the Prophet, it is valid without mahr; for others, mahr is obligatory. Identify the proof from 'ilaqah sababiyyah.
Text: 'وكذا نکاح غيره عندنا'.
2
Identify the Shafi'i position and its first proof
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that marriage with hibah is only valid for the Prophet. His first proof is the Quranic verse 'Khalisatan laka' (pure for you), which he interprets as making the word specific to the Prophet.
Text: 'وعند الشافعي ... بلکہ صرف لفظ نکاح یا تزویج سے منعقد ہوگا کیونکہ اللہ تعالٰی خالصتہ لک فرما دیا ہے'.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to the first proof
The Hanafi response is twofold: (1) The exclusivity is in the ruling (no mahr), not the word. (2) The verse can refer to the exclusivity of the Prophet's wives to him. Thus, the word itself is not restricted.
Text: 'قلنا المخلوص في الحکم ...' and 'دایضا يحتمل ...'.
4
Analyze the Shafi'i second proof and Hanafi response
The second Shafi'i proof: marriage is a contract for numerous interests that only the words nikah and tazwij can indicate. The Hanafi response: the primary purpose of marriage is ownership, and the interests are subsidiary. Since ownership is the core, words indicating ownership (like hibah) are appropriate.
Text: 'ولانه عقد شرع یه ...' and Hanafi response 'نکاح کے مشروعیتہ اور وضع ان مصالح کے لئے نہیں بلکہ ...'.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: a marriage contracted with the word hibah is valid according to Hanafis (with mahr), but invalid according to Shafi'is. This affects the legal status of such marriages.

خالصتہ لک کا دوسرا جواب ہے ۔ حاصل یہ کہ اس آیت کا مطلب یہ بھی ہو سکتا ہے۔ رواللہ اعلم بمراده بذالک) حاصل یہ کہ خالصتہ لک ا تعلق انا امللنا الک ازواجک کے ساتھ ہے اور ترکیبی حیثیت سے ازواجک سے حال ہے ۔ معنی یہ ہو گا انا اطلا لک از واجک حال کو نہا خالصته لک یعنی ازواج نبی علیہ السلام رسول اللہ صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم کے ساتھ مختص ہیں ۔ اور کسی شخص کے لئے ان کے ساتھ نکاح کرنا حلال نہیں ہے ۔ جیسے اللہ تعالیٰ نے ان کے حق میں فرمایا ہے و ازواجہ امھا تھم ، خواہ نبی اپنی زندگی میں خدانخواستہ طلاق دے دے اور اسی طرح بعد الوفات بھی ان ازواج مطہرات کا نکاح کسی سے نہیں ہو سکتا ۔ تو یہ ازواج مطہرات فی حیواۃ النبی اور بعد وفات النبی انھیں کے لئے خالص مخلص ہیں۔ لاني اللفظ یعنی خلوص و اختصاص لفظ میں نہیں۔ کیونکہ مجاز حضرت رسالہ علیہ السلام کے ساتھ مختص ہیں ہے کیونکہ یہ تو ایک لسانی اور لفظی مسئلہ ہے ۔ اس کا کسی کے ساتھ اختصاص نہیں ہوسکتا۔ وايضا تلك الامور الجنسية امام شافعی کی دوسری دلیل که مشروعیتہ نکاح مصالح کے لئے ہے۔ یہ اس کا جواب ہے . حاصل جواب یہ کہ نکاح کے مشروعیتہ اور وضع ان مصالح کے لئے نہیں بلکہ یہ نکاح کے فروع و ثمرات ہیں۔ اور نکاح کی بنا اور وضع اس کے لئے ہے کہ خاوند کو منکوحہ پر ملک حاصل ہو جائے ۔ یہی وجہ ہے کہ خاوند کے ذمہ بعوض ملک نکاح مہر لازم ہو جاتی ہے اور طلاق بھی خاوند کے قبضہ میں ہے ۔ تو یہ صاف دلیل ہے کہ وہ مالک ہے اور عقد نکاح برائے اثبات ملک ہے۔ لوکان وضعہ اگر وضع نکاح ان مصالح کی خاطر ہوتی تو یہ تمام مصالح مشترک بین الزوجین ہیں تو پھر مهر بحق زوجہ واجب على الزوج نہ ہوتی اور ملک طلاق صرف زوج کے ساتھ مخصوص نہ ہوتا ۔ فاذا كان الخ تو جب مہر زوج پر واجب ہے اور طلاق بھی اس کے ملک و قبضہ میں ہے تو معلوم ہوا کہ نکاح کی وضع ملک الزوج علی الزوجتہ کے لئے ہے ۔ وانا صح بلفظین جب نکاح ان دو لفظوں کے ساتھ صحیح ہے جو لغتہ وال علی الملک نہیں ہیں یعنی لفظ نکاح و تزویج فاولى ان يصح تو پھر ان الفاظ کے ساتھ جو دال علی الملک ہیں جیسے لفظ بہ وغیرہ ان کے ساتھ انعقاد نکاح بطریق اولی صحیح ہونا چاہتے . وانما يصح بهما أى يصح النكاح بلفظ النكاح والتزويج لإنهما صارا علمين لهذا العقد الى عقد النكاح جواب اشکال یعنی وانما یصح الخریہ ایک اشکال کا جواب ہے ۔ اشکال یہ کہ جب لفظ نكاح وتزويج لخته دال علی الملک نہیں ہیں تو پھر ان لفظوں کے ساتھ نکاح صحیح نہ ہونا چاہئے کیونکہ ان کی ملک متد پر نہ دلالت ہے نہ وضع تو اس کا جواب دیا کہ یہ دونوں لفظ یعنی لفظ نکاح اور تزویج اس عقد شرعی کے لئے بمنزلہ علم کے ہو چکے ہیں یعنی جیسے علم علی التعیین مسمی کے لئے موضوع ہوتا ہے۔ اسی طرح شرعا یہ الفاظ اس عقد کے لئے موضوع ہو چکے ہیں اور اعلام میں معنی لغوی کی رعایتہ لازمی نہیں ہے ۔ وکذ ا ینعقد النكاح اس
There are three conditions. And the clear necessary element for legal acts: the necessary element of legal acts is both textual and meaningful, which is outside their meaning. However, it applies to legal acts that from the conception of legal acts, this meaning necessarily comes to mind. This is the same meaning of legitimacy. And 'sababiyyah' (causality) is an 'atf' (conjunction) on his statement 'ittisal al-j'? Its 'atf' is 'ittisal' on 'fi ma'na al-mashru'iyyah'. And 'ittisal fi ma'na al-mashru'iyyah' was an 'ilaqah mushabahah' (relation of similarity), and in legal matters for majaz, the relation of similarity is also effective. 'Ka nikahihi 'alayhi al-salam' etc. This is an example of 'ilaqah sababiyyah'. That is, the marriage of the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, is contracted with the word hibah: when a free woman says, 'I gift myself to you, O Messenger of Allah,' and the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, accepts it, then the marriage is contracted: 'Indeed, she offered herself to the Prophet.' So the word hibah is placed in reality for ownership of the corpus, and the placement of marriage is for ownership of usufruct. That is, ownership of the corpus is the cause of ownership of usufruct. For example, when a slave-girl comes into the ownership of a person, ownership of usufruct also accrues to him, and intercourse with an owned slave-girl is legally permissible. So if the word that was placed for ownership of the corpus is applied and ownership of usufruct is intended, then with the relation of causality this majaz is valid. Likewise, the marriage of others according to us: According to us, the marriage of persons other than the Prophet, peace be upon him, is also contracted with the word hibah with the relation of causality, if the woman is free. Because if the woman is a slave-girl and her owner says 'I gift her to you', then according to us 'asabah' is established and ownership of the corpus is obtained. If the woman is free and she or her guardian says 'I gift myself to you' or 'I gift my ward to you' and the addressee accepts, then the marriage is contracted. According to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, the marriage of someone other than the Prophet, peace be upon him, is not contracted by the word hibah, but only by the word nikah or tazwij, because Allah Most High said 'Khalisatan laka' (pure for you), so it is understood that the conclusion of marriage with the word hibah is specific to the Prophet, peace be upon him. And also because it is a legal contract: Imam Shafi'i's second proof is that the marriage contract is a contract whose legitimacy is for innumerable interests, such as establishing lineage, continuity of the human race, abstinence from adultery/fornication, attaining ihsan, creating affection between the two, mutual assistance in livelihood, and many other benefits too numerous to count. Other than the two words (nikah and tazwij) are incapable of indicating these interests, so marriage is contracted only with these two words. Other words are incapable of indication, so marriage is not contracted with them. We say: 'Al-makhlas fi al-hukm' – this is the response to the first proof of 'Khalisatan laka', i.e., the ruling of hibah is specific to the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, meaning marriage without dower is contracted... The verse means that the validity of marriage with the word hibah without obligation of dower is specific to you. As for persons other than the Prophet, peace be upon him, their marriage with the word hibah is contracted, but dower becomes obligatory. Also, it is possible: this is the second response to 'Khalisatan laka'. The meaning of the verse can also be that 'Khalisatan laka' is related to 'our giving you your wives' and grammatically it is a hal (circumstantial accusative) from 'azwajik'. The meaning would be: 'We have given you your wives, while they are pure for you', i.e., the wives of the Prophet are specific to him and it is not permissible for anyone else to marry them, as Allah said about them: 'and his wives are their mothers', whether the Prophet divorces them during his lifetime (God forbid) or after his death. So these pure wives are purely exclusive to him during the Prophet's life and after his death. Because exclusivity is not in the word. Because majaz is specific to the Prophet? Rather, this is a linguistic and verbal issue; it cannot be specific to anyone. And also those generic matters: this is a response to Imam Shafi'i's second proof that the legitimacy of marriage is for interests. The essence of the response: The legitimacy and placement of marriage is not for these interests; rather, these are branches and fruits of marriage. The foundation and placement of marriage is for the husband to acquire ownership over the wife. This is why the husband is obligated to pay dower in exchange for ownership in marriage, and divorce is in the husband's control. This is clear evidence that he is owner and the marriage contract is for establishing ownership. If its placement were for these interests, then all these interests are common between the spouses; then dower would not be obligatory on the husband in favor of the wife, and the right of divorce would not be specific to the husband. So since dower is obligatory on the husband and divorce is in his ownership and control, it is understood that the placement of marriage is for the husband's ownership over the wife. And since marriage is valid with two words that are not indicative of ownership in language, i.e., the words nikah and tazwij, then it is more appropriate that it should be valid with words that indicate ownership, like the word hibah, etc. It is valid with them: the marriage is valid with the words nikah and tazwij because they have become technical terms for this contract. This is a response to an objection: the objection being that if the words nikah and tazwij are not indicative of ownership, then marriage should not be valid with them because they do not indicate ownership of usufruct either in language or placement. The response: these two words have become like proper nouns for this legal contract; just as a proper noun is placed for a specific named entity, similarly, in Shariah, these words have been placed for this contract, and in proper nouns, consideration of the literal meaning is not necessary. And similarly, marriage is contracted...
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul discussion on the types of 'ilaqat (relations) that make majaz valid, specifically 'ilaqah sababiyyah (causal relation), and its application to the ruling of contracting marriage with the word hibah (gift). The author begins by explaining that for legal acts, there is a necessary meaning that comes to mind; this meaning is the meaning of legitimacy. Then he discusses the conjunction of 'ilaqah sababiyyah, noting that previously they discussed 'ilaqah mushabahah (similarity), but here the focus is on 'ilaqah sababiyyah, which is also effective for majaz in legal matters. He gives the example of the Prophet's marriage: when a woman gifted herself to the Prophet using the word 'hibah', the marriage was valid. The reason is that hibah literally means transfer of ownership of the corpus (milk al-raqabah), but marriage involves ownership of usufruct (milk al-mut'ah). Since ownership of the corpus is a cause for ownership of usufruct (as in the case of a slave-girl where owning her allows intercourse), using the word for the cause (hibah) to mean the effect (marriage) is a valid majaz with causal relation. The author then extends this to others: according to the Hanafi school, the marriage of anyone other than the Prophet is also valid with the word hibah, provided the woman is free. If the woman is a slave, her master saying 'I gift her to you' establishes ownership, which is not marriage but a transfer of slaves. For a free woman, her offer of 'hibah' and the man's acceptance constitutes a valid marriage contract, with the obligation of mahr (dower). Then the author presents the Shafi'i position: Imam al-Shafi'i holds that marriage with the word hibah is only for the Prophet, based on the Quranic verse 'Khalisatan laka' (pure for you) and on the nature of marriage as a contract for numerous interests that only the specific words of nikah and tazwij can fulfill. The author provides two Hanafi responses: (1) The exclusivity in the verse refers to the ruling of marriage without dower, not to the validity of using the word hibah. The Prophet's marriage with hibah is valid without mahr, but for others, mahr is obligatory. Alternatively, the verse is about the exclusivity of the Prophet's wives to him, not about the word. (2) The interests attributed to marriage (lineage, etc.) are not the primary purpose of the contract; rather, the primary purpose is to establish the husband's ownership over the wife. This is proven by the fact that dower is obligatory on the husband and divorce is in his hand. If the contract were for mutual interests, these rights would be symmetrical. Since they are asymmetrical, the contract is for ownership. Therefore, words that indicate ownership (like hibah) are even more appropriate than words that do not (like nikah and tazwij, which are technical terms). The passage ends by addressing the objection that nikah and tazwij are valid even though they do not indicate ownership; the response is that they have become technical terms for the contract, so their literal meaning is not required.

Key Terms
علاقہ سببیہ ('ilaqah sababiyyah): A relation where one thing is the cause of another; used for valid majaz when the word for the cause is used to mean the effect.
مجاز (majaz): Use of a word in a non-literal sense because of a connection ('ilaqah) between the literal and intended meanings.
ملک الرقبہ (milk al-raqabah): Ownership of the corpus (the thing itself), as opposed to ownership of usufruct.
ملک المتعہ (milk al-mut'ah): Ownership of usufruct (the right to benefit from something, e.g., sexual enjoyment in marriage).

Key Terms from Source Passage

What is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of the dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is on marriage with lafz hibah?
A) Hanafis require mahr, Shafi'is do not.
B) Hanafis consider it valid for all, Shafi'is consider it invalid for non-Prophet individuals.
C) Both schools agree it is valid but differ on the required witnesses.
D) Shafi'is allow it only for slaves, Hanafis for free women.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on marriage with lafz hibah revolves around the interpretation of 'Khalisatan laka' and the primary purpose of marriage, with Hanafis allowing it for all via 'ilaqah sababiyyah.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
12 min
Derived Terms and Metaphorical Transfer in Hanafi Usul
Usul rule: conditions for reciprocal metaphorical transfer and distinction between literal and conventional derived terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that isti'arah min al-tarafayn is conditional on the cause being legislated for the effect, and distinguish between literal and conventional derived terms in legal rulings.

Core Idea
A cause-effect metaphor works both ways only when the cause is legislated for the effect; otherwise, linguistic convention determines the ruling.

In the previous lesson, we saw how the word 'hiba' can validly contract marriage due to a cause-effect relationship. This lesson expands that principle to a general usul rule: when can we metaphorically apply the name of a cause to intend its effect, and vice versa? The text presents an objection and its answer, culminating in a concrete fiqh example involving conditional manumission that illustrates the crucial distinction between literal and conventional derived terms.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Cause-Effect Relationship
Identify the alaqah sababiyyah between cause (e.g., sale) and effect (e.g., ownership). The author shows that applying the cause name to intend the effect is generally valid (e.g., using 'bay' to mean marriage).
2
Recognize the Objection and the Condition
The objection demands symmetry: if cause→effect is valid, why not effect→cause? The answer introduces a condition: reciprocal metaphor is valid only when the cause is legislated specifically to bring about that effect. Sale is legislated for ownership; ownership of corpus (milk raqabah) is not legislated for ownership of enjoyment (milk mut'ah).
This condition is the key to the rule.
3
Analyze the Slave Manumission Example
Two conditional expressions: 'If I own a slave' (in mamlakat) vs 'If I buy a slave' (in ishtaraytu). In the first, buying half and then later buying the other half after selling the first half does not trigger manumission because the seller is not considered 'owner' at the time. In the second, the same sequence triggers manumission because the seller is customarily called 'buyer' (mushtari).
Focus on why the legal outcome differs despite identical actions.
4
Distinguish Between Literal and Conventional Derived Terms
The principle: a derived term (mushtaqq) like 'malik' is used literally only when the derived-from meaning (ownership) is present. After removal of ownership, its use is metaphorical. However, 'mushtari' has become a conventional reality (haqiqah 'urfiyyah) – the term is applied even after the act of buying ends. This explains the difference in manumission rulings.
Convention (urf) can elevate a metaphorical usage to the status of reality in legal language.

عید کے ساتھ متصف ہوگا۔ کیونکہ عرفاً اسے مشتری عبید کہا جائیگا۔ وهذا ابناء على ان يه مسئله در حقیقت اس ضابطہ پر مبنی ہے کہ صفات مشتقه مثلاً اسم فاعل اور اسم مفعول وصفته ساتھ قائم ہے تو یہ اطلاق بطور لیکن بعض صورتوں میں یہ مجاز لغوی حقیقتہ عرفیہ بن چکا ہے اور لفظ مشتری اسی قبیلے سے ہے کہ جب فعل شراء سے فارغ ہو جائے تو اہل عرف اسے مشتری کہہ دیتے ہیں۔ تو لفظ مشتری اس صورت میں منقول عرفی ہو گیا جو بدرجہ حقیقتہ عرفیہ ہے ۔ اما لفظ المالک اور لفظ ممالک کا اطلاق بعد از زوال ملک اطلاق نہیں کیا جاتا نہ عرفا نہ لفظہ ۔ تو اس کا اطلاق بعد از زوال ملک ہر صورت میں مجاز ہوگا تو ان مملکت کی صورت میں حقیقتہ لغویہ مراد ہوگی تو نصف اول فروخت کر دینے کے بعد جب نصف ثانی اس نے خرید لی تو یہ مالک ہونے کے ساتھ لغتہ متصف نہیں ہے۔ لہذا عتق نہیں ہوگا۔ اور اشتریت کی صورت میں حقیقتہ عرفیہ مراد ہوگی تو نصف اول کے فروخت کے بعد جب نصف ثانی خرید لی تو اہل عرف کے نزدیک یہ مشتری عبد کہلائیگا ۔ اس لئے نصف ثانی ازاد ہو جائے گی
The 'atf of 'ibada is on 'gada nikah ghairuhu 'indana'. That is, just as the contract of marriage is concluded with the word 'hiba', similarly marriage will be concluded with the word 'bay' (sale). For example, a free, sane, adult woman says: 'I have given myself to you in marriage' and the addressee accepts, then the marriage is contracted. Here also the cause-effect relationship (alaqah sababiyyah) is working: the placement of 'bay' is for ownership of corpus (milk raqabah), and this is a cause for ownership of enjoyment (milk mut'ah). So the application of the cause (the word 'bay') intends the effect (ownership of enjoyment), which is the marriage contract. If it is said: it would necessitate the opposite. The summary of the objection: then on the basis of this cause-effect relationship, the opposite should also be established—that is, applying the name of the effect and intending the cause is correct, so applying the word 'nikah' and intending 'bay' or 'hiba' would be correct by way of applying the effect to the cause. Since the placement of 'nikah' is for ownership of enjoyment, which is the effect of ownership of corpus, therefore mentioning the effect (ownership of enjoyment) and intending the cause (ownership of corpus) should be correct. We say: it is only so when the cause is legislated for the effect; the summary of the answer: the application of the name of the effect to the cause is correct only when that cause is legislated for the ruling, i.e., this cause was legislated so that from it this effect is established as the primary objective. The summary: from the legislation of the cause, the primary objective is that very ruling which is the effect. Like sale for ownership: sale is a cause legislated for ownership, and ownership is the effect. It is the primary objective thereof, because ownership is the ultimate cause for sale. In such a place, metaphor from both sides (isti'arah min al-tarafayn) is correct. Contrary to ownership of corpus: its primary objective from its legislation is not ownership of enjoyment, nor was ownership of corpus placed for achieving it. For example, a slave or a Magian or a Wathaniyyah slave girl can be owned, but here ownership of enjoyment is not obtained. So it is known that ownership of corpus is not a cause that is legislated for the effect, i.e., ownership of enjoyment. In such places, metaphor from both sides cannot apply. In the next issue there is more clarification of this answer. Ponder over it; here it is: So he said: 'If I own a slave now...' That is, a person says: 'If I own a slave' or says: 'If I buy a slave, then he is free.' Then he bought it in parts: for example, half today and the other half on another day. In the case of saying 'I bought', the slave becomes free, and in the first case (saying 'I own'), he does not become free. Elaborating, he said: In the case of 'if I own a slave', he bought half of the slave and sold it, then later bought the other half. In this case, the second half will not be free because the condition of owning the complete slave is not fulfilled, since after selling the first half, when he buys the second half, he is not described as owning the slave. So he will not be free. And if he said 'if I buy a slave' and bought half and then bought the second half, the second half will be free because by purchasing, he becomes described as 'buyer of the slave' (mushtari 'abd). Because customarily he is called a buyer of slaves. And this issue is based on the principle that derived attributes (like ism fa'il and ism maf'ul and adjectives) when the derived-from meaning (mushtaq minhu) is present, the application is in the literal sense. But after the removal of the derived-from meaning, that is, he was described with the derived-from meaning, but now that derived-from has been removed, then in that case the application of the derived term is linguistic metaphor. However, in some forms, this linguistic metaphor has become conventional reality (haqiqah 'urfiyyah). The word 'mushtari' is of this category: when the act of buying (shira') ends, the people of custom call him 'mushtari'. So the word 'mushtari' in this case has become a conventional transfer (manqul 'urfi) which is at the level of conventional reality. As for the word 'al-malik' and 'al-mamlak', their application after the removal of ownership is not made, neither customarily nor linguistically. So its application after the removal of ownership is in any case metaphorical. So in the case of 'in mamlakat' (if I owned), the literal linguistic reality is intended. So after selling the first half and then buying the second half, he is not linguistically described as owning, so manumission does not occur. And in the case of 'ishtaraytu' (I bought), the conventional reality is intended. So after selling the first half and then buying the second half, in the view of the people of custom, he is called 'mushtari' (buyer) of the slave, so the second half becomes free.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul analysis of the use of derived terms (mushtaqq) and metaphorical transfer (isti'arah) in legal language. The author begins by revisiting the earlier discussion of using the word 'hiba' for marriage, establishing that a cause-effect relationship (alaqah sababiyyah) allows applying the name of the cause to intend the effect. He then addresses an objection: if that is allowed, why cannot the opposite be allowed—applying the name of the effect to the cause? The answer is conditional: it is only valid when the cause is legislated specifically to bring about that effect. For example, sale (bay') is legislated for ownership, so applying 'sale' to mean 'ownership' is valid, and vice versa. But ownership of corpus (milk raqabah) is not legislated for ownership of enjoyment (milk mut'ah); thus the reverse is not valid. The author then presents a concrete fiqh example: conditional manumission. If someone says, 'If I own a slave, he is free' and buys the slave in parts, the second half is not freed because he is not considered 'owner' after selling the first half. But if he says, 'If I buy a slave, he is free,' the second half is freed because customarily he remains a 'buyer' even after selling part. The principle is that derived terms like 'malik' are literal only when the derived-from meaning (ownership) persists, while 'mushtari' has become a transferred conventional term applied even after the act of buying is complete. This illustrates the Hanafi usul hierarchy of literal and metaphorical usage.

Key Terms
علاقة سببية (alaqah sababiyyah): The relationship between cause and effect that justifies applying the name of one to intend the other.
استعارة من الطرفين (isti'arah min al-tarafayn): Metaphor from both sides: using the name of the cause for the effect and vice versa, valid only when the cause is legislated for that effect.
مشتق (mushtaqq): Derived term (like اسم فاعل or اسم مفعول) whose literal use requires the derived-from meaning to be present; after removal it becomes مجاز unless it has become عرفي.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the slave manumission example, why does the second half become free when the condition is 'If I buy a slave' but not when it is 'If I own a slave'?
A) Because 'buying' is a more specific act than 'owning'.
B) Because 'mushtari' has become a conventional term applied even after the act of buying, while 'malik' remains literal and requires continuous ownership.
C) Because the first half was sold before buying the second half.
D) Because the intention of the speaker changes the ruling.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of reciprocal metaphorical transfer in legal language depends on whether the cause is legislated for the effect; derived terms may become conventional, altering their legal application.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
Conditions for Valid Isti'ara in Usul al-Fiqh
Analyzing Hanafi view on isti'ara of talaq for izalat al-milk

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi conditions for valid isti'ara to evaluate whether a metaphorical usage is permissible between two legal terms.

Core Idea
Isti'ara requires the source attribute to be stronger and the target to be a necessary consequence.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, metaphorical usage (isti'ara) is governed by strict conditions. This lesson examines the dispute over whether the term talaq (divorce) can be used metaphorically for 'itaq (manumission) to mean removal of ownership. You will learn why the Hanafi school argues that such a metaphor fails because the source attribute is weaker and the connection is not necessary, reinforcing the precision required in legal language.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim and counterclaim
Determine who is arguing for and against the metaphorical use of talaq for removal of ownership, and state each position.
The opposition claims talaq can be used metaphorically because both involve removal; Hanafis disagree.
2
Extract the Hanafi conditions for isti'ara
From the text, list the two conditions: (1) the source (musta'ar minhu) must be stronger in the shared attribute, (2) the target (musta'ar lahu) must be a necessary consequence of the source.
These are derived from the analogy with 'lion' and 'bravery'.
3
Apply the conditions to the disputed case
Check if removal of restraint (talaq) is stronger in removal than removal of ownership, and whether removal of ownership is a necessary consequence of divorce.
The text concludes removal of ownership is stronger and not a necessary consequence.
4
Determine the valid direction of metaphor
Based on the conditions, decide which direction is valid: using 'itaq for talaq (stronger to weaker) or using talaq for 'itaq (weaker to stronger).
Only the direction from stronger to weaker (izalat al-milk for izalat al-qayd) is valid.
5
Note the practical implication for legal reasoning
Understand that this ruling prevents conflating categories of talaq and 'itaq, ensuring precise application of legal terms.
This affects how analogies (qiyas) are drawn between different acts.
