And this waw also becomes a deputy for the agent, meaning the agent of the conjunct is separate and the waw stands in its place. It is possible that the intended meaning is 'or he does not do the totality'. This meaning applies when the waw is for following. So according to this probability, breaking the oath does not occur by doing one act, but by committing the totality. And it is also possible that the intended meaning is 'he does not do'. This is the meaning when the waw is a deputy for the agent. Then the oath becomes multiple. So he breaks the oath by doing each one individually. When both types of probability exist in the text, then preference is needed based on contextual indication (dalalat al-haal). Since the Shariah has forbidden both zina and consuming the property of an orphan, each individually, therefore he breaks the oath by doing one as well. And sometimes it (aw) is for permissibility (ibaha), like 'sit with the fuqaha or the muhaddithin'. This 'aw' is for permissibility, not for choice (takhyir). The difference between ibaha and takhyir is that in takhyir, combining the two is not possible; only one is to be chosen. In contrast, ibaha allows combining. In the mentioned example, the addressee can sit with both fuqaha and muhaddithin. In summary, takhyir means prevention of combination (man' al-jam'), i.e., both cannot come together, though being devoid of both is not harmful. In contrast, ibaha means prevention of being devoid (man' al-khulu), i.e., being devoid of both is not allowed, but combining both is correct. In the example, it is not correct to abandon sitting with both fuqaha and muhaddithin; rather it is correct to choose to sit with both. It is known by contextual indication (dalalat al-haal) whether the intended meaning is takhyir or ibaha. Based on this, they (the jurists) said that in 'I will not speak to anyone except so-and-so or so-and-so', the 'aw' is for ibaha; the person can speak to both. Therefore, here 'aw' is for prevention of being devoid, not for prevention of combination. Because the exception (istithna) is after prohibition (an-nahy). The summary is that this exception is after prohibition, meaning after prevention and obstacle. First he said 'I will not speak', then he made an exception, and an exception after prohibition according to the rule is for ibaha, so here it is ibaha. And sometimes 'aw' is metaphorically used for 'hatta' (until). This is when a mansub mudari' occurs after 'aw' and there is no mansub mudari' before it, or even if a mudari' is present but not mansub, rather preceding it is an extended matter whose interruption is intended by the action that occurs after 'aw'. In such cases, conjunction is absolutely not possible; it is taken in the meaning of 'hatta'. Just as what precedes 'hatta' is the co-existent thing (mu'ayya) and what follows 'hatta' is its limit (ghayah), so with the existence of the limit, the co-existent thing is removed. Here it is similar because one of the two conjuncts is removed by the existence of the other. Due to this correspondence, 'aw' takes the meaning of 'hatta', as in 'I will certainly stay with you or you give me my right'. Here the staying is extended until the payment of the right occurs, then the staying is removed, just as the co-existent thing is removed by the existence of the limit. Now in the Quranic verse there is an objection: The verse 'You have no part in the matter, or He turns to them (in mercy) or punishes them' – although this is extended, can it be removed when 'or He turns to them' or 'or punishes them' occurs? That is, when Allah grants them success to accept Islam, or causes them to die in a state of shirk and punishes them, would 'You have no part in the matter' end and the matter come into the Prophet's control? Whereas it is not so, as Allah says 'To Allah belongs the matter before and after', and the end of the verse 'and to Allah belongs whatever is in the heavens and whatever is on the earth' indicates that this matter is in Allah's possession and control at all times. To remove this objection, two explanations are given: First, when the Prophet (peace be upon him) was wounded in the Battle of Uhud, in extreme distress he said, 'How can a people succeed who have wounded their Prophet?' and also said, 'O Allah, curse Abu Sufyan, Harith ibn Hisham, Suhayl ibn Amr, and Safwan ibn Umayyah'. This was in a state of extreme distress. So Allah said, 'You have no part in the matter, or He turns to them (in mercy) or punishes them'. Through these two possibilities, the distress was removed. The meaning is: 'until He turns to them and you are happy with their state, or He punishes them and you are avenged from them.' So 'You have no part' indicated the anxiety and distress which is removed by what comes after 'aw', just as the co-existent thing is removed by the limit. Thus 'aw' is in the meaning of 'hatta'. Second, that with 'You have no part in the matter', the supplication for the destruction of enemies was prohibited, which was likely to be extended. Therefore 'aw' is interpreted in the sense of a limit (ghayah), just as an extended matter is concluded with its limit. Here it is similar, because if they become Muslims, the curse does not occur; if they are destroyed, the objective is achieved; there is no need for the curse nor for its extension. And Allah knows best. If someone swears, 'I will not enter this house or I enter that house', then in this statement, after 'aw' the word 'adkhulu' is a mansub mudari', and before it there is no mansub mudari'. Therefore conjunction is not possible. In this case, the non-entry of the first house is extended until he enters the second. If he enters the first, he breaks the oath because in this scenario, the non-entry of the first is
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the Arabic particle 'aw' (أو) and its various legal-linguistic functions within Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author examines four distinct usages: (1) as a conjunctive particle in oaths, where depending on whether it acts as a substitute for the verb (na'ib 'an al-'amil) or simply links, breaking the oath may require committing all acts or just any one; this leads to a contextual determination because Shariah prohibits each offense individually. (2) For permissibility (ibaha) versus choice (takhyir): the key distinction is that ibaha allows gathering all options, while takhyir forces a choice of only one. The example 'sit with the fuqaha or the muhaddithin' illustrates ibaha because one may sit with both. The rule is that an exception after a prohibition indicates ibaha, not choice. (3) Metaphorical use as 'hatta' (until) when a accusative present tense verb follows 'aw' and no accusative present tense precedes; then 'aw' indicates the endpoint of a continuous state, e.g., 'I will certainly stay with you or you give me my right' means 'until you give me my right'. (4) Application to the Qur'anic verse 'ليس لك من الأمر شيء أو يتوب عليهم أو يعذبهم' (Qur'an 3:128): an objection arises that the verse seems to end the Prophet's involvement in the matter when either mercy or punishment occurs, yet the matter is always Allah's. Two resolutions are offered: first, the Prophet's distress (from being wounded at Uhud) is removed by Allah's turning or punishing; second, the prohibition of cursing was temporary, and 'aw' means 'until', so the matter ends with either outcome but remains Allah's. Finally, an oath example: 'I will not enter this house or I enter that house' – since 'aw' is followed by an accusative present verb without a preceding accusative, conjunction is impossible; the oath means the non-entry of the first lasts until entry of the second, so entering the first directly breaks the oath.

Key Terms
أو (aw): A particle with multiple meanings: conjunction, permissibility (ibaha), choice (takhyir), or metaphorical use as 'hatta' (until), determined by context.
نائب عن العامل (na'ib 'an al-'amil): Deputy for the verb: the particle stands in place of the verb's agent, making the object of the oath multiple.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, 'aw' can require either a single act or the totality, resolved by contextual indication (dalalat al-haal).

Ibaha vs. Takhyir and 'Aw' as 'Hatta'
Analysis of ibaha, takhyir, and 'aw' as 'hatta'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ibaha (permissibility) and takhyir (choice) when interpreting 'aw', apply the rule that exception after prohibition indicates ibaha, and identify when 'aw' serves as 'hatta' (until) based on grammatical and contextual markers.

Core Idea
Exception after prohibition makes 'aw' indicate permissibility, not choice; and 'aw' can metaphorically mean 'until' when followed by a mansub mudari'.

Beyond oaths, the particle 'aw' is used in permissibility statements and can even take on the meaning of 'hatta' (until). Hanafi usul sets clear criteria to distinguish permissibility (ibaha) from choice (takhyir) and to recognize when 'aw' indicates a limit. This lesson examines these uses with examples from the Qur'an and everyday oaths.

Guided Path
1
Determine if 'aw' is for ibaha or takhyir
Ask: does the context allow combining all options? If yes, it is ibaha (prevention of being devoid). If it forces selection of only one, it is takhyir (prevention of combination).
The example 'sit with the fuqaha or muhaddithin' is ibaha because you can sit with both.
2
Apply the rule of exception after prohibition
When an exception (istithna) follows a prohibition, it indicates ibaha. E.g., 'I will not speak to anyone except Zaid or Amr' – the exception allows speaking to both.
This is a key Hanafi principle.
3
Identify when 'aw' is used as 'hatta'
If a mansub mudari' follows 'aw' and there is no preceding mansub mudari', and the context involves an extended state, then 'aw' means 'until'. The state continues until the action occurs.
Example: 'I will stay with you or you give me my right' means until you give me my right.
4
Resolve the Quranic objection (3:128)
In 'You have no part in the matter, or He turns to them or punishes them', 'aw' is interpreted as 'hatta' to show that the Prophet's distress ends with either outcome, while the matter remains Allah's.
Two explanations are given: the Prophet's distress is removed, or the prohibition of cursing is limited.

And this waw also becomes a deputy for the agent, meaning the agent of the conjunct is separate and the waw stands in its place. It is possible that the intended meaning is 'or he does not do the totality'. This meaning applies when the waw is for following. So according to this probability, breaking the oath does not occur by doing one act, but by committing the totality. And it is also possible that the intended meaning is 'he does not do'. This is the meaning when the waw is a deputy for the agent. Then the oath becomes multiple. So he breaks the oath by doing each one individually. When both types of probability exist in the text, then preference is needed based on contextual indication (dalalat al-haal). Since the Shariah has forbidden both zina and consuming the property of an orphan, each individually, therefore he breaks the oath by doing one as well. And sometimes it (aw) is for permissibility (ibaha), like 'sit with the fuqaha or the muhaddithin'. This 'aw' is for permissibility, not for choice (takhyir). The difference between ibaha and takhyir is that in takhyir, combining the two is not possible; only one is to be chosen. In contrast, ibaha allows combining. In the mentioned example, the addressee can sit with both fuqaha and muhaddithin. In summary, takhyir means prevention of combination (man' al-jam'), i.e., both cannot come together, though being devoid of both is not harmful. In contrast, ibaha means prevention of being devoid (man' al-khulu), i.e., being devoid of both is not allowed, but combining both is correct. In the example, it is not correct to abandon sitting with both fuqaha and muhaddithin; rather it is correct to choose to sit with both. It is known by contextual indication (dalalat al-haal) whether the intended meaning is takhyir or ibaha. Based on this, they (the jurists) said that in 'I will not speak to anyone except so-and-so or so-and-so', the 'aw' is for ibaha; the person can speak to both. Therefore, here 'aw' is for prevention of being devoid, not for prevention of combination. Because the exception (istithna) is after prohibition (an-nahy). The summary is that this exception is after prohibition, meaning after prevention and obstacle. First he said 'I will not speak', then he made an exception, and an exception after prohibition according to the rule is for ibaha, so here it is ibaha. And sometimes 'aw' is metaphorically used for 'hatta' (until). This is when a mansub mudari' occurs after 'aw' and there is no mansub mudari' before it, or even if a mudari' is present but not mansub, rather preceding it is an extended matter whose interruption is intended by the action that occurs after 'aw'. In such cases, conjunction is absolutely not possible; it is taken in the meaning of 'hatta'. Just as what precedes 'hatta' is the co-existent thing (mu'ayya) and what follows 'hatta' is its limit (ghayah), so with the existence of the limit, the co-existent thing is removed. Here it is similar because one of the two conjuncts is removed by the existence of the other. Due to this correspondence, 'aw' takes the meaning of 'hatta', as in 'I will certainly stay with you or you give me my right'. Here the staying is extended until the payment of the right occurs, then the staying is removed, just as the co-existent thing is removed by the existence of the limit. Now in the Quranic verse there is an objection: The verse 'You have no part in the matter, or He turns to them (in mercy) or punishes them' – although this is extended, can it be removed when 'or He turns to them' or 'or punishes them' occurs? That is, when Allah grants them success to accept Islam, or causes them to die in a state of shirk and punishes them, would 'You have no part in the matter' end and the matter come into the Prophet's control? Whereas it is not so, as Allah says 'To Allah belongs the matter before and after', and the end of the verse 'and to Allah belongs whatever is in the heavens and whatever is on the earth' indicates that this matter is in Allah's possession and control at all times. To remove this objection, two explanations are given: First, when the Prophet (peace be upon him) was wounded in the Battle of Uhud, in extreme distress he said, 'How can a people succeed who have wounded their Prophet?' and also said, 'O Allah, curse Abu Sufyan, Harith ibn Hisham, Suhayl ibn Amr, and Safwan ibn Umayyah'. This was in a state of extreme distress. So Allah said, 'You have no part in the matter, or He turns to them (in mercy) or punishes them'. Through these two possibilities, the distress was removed. The meaning is: 'until He turns to them and you are happy with their state, or He punishes them and you are avenged from them.' So 'You have no part' indicated the anxiety and distress which is removed by what comes after 'aw', just as the co-existent thing is removed by the limit. Thus 'aw' is in the meaning of 'hatta'. Second, that with 'You have no part in the matter', the supplication for the destruction of enemies was prohibited, which was likely to be extended. Therefore 'aw' is interpreted in the sense of a limit (ghayah), just as an extended matter is concluded with its limit. Here it is similar, because if they become Muslims, the curse does not occur; if they are destroyed, the objective is achieved; there is no need for the curse nor for its extension. And Allah knows best. If someone swears, 'I will not enter this house or I enter that house', then in this statement, after 'aw' the word 'adkhulu' is a mansub mudari', and before it there is no mansub mudari'. Therefore conjunction is not possible. In this case, the non-entry of the first house is extended until he enters the second. If he enters the first, he breaks the oath because in this scenario, the non-entry of the first is

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the Arabic particle 'aw' (أو) and its various legal-linguistic functions within Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author examines four distinct usages: (1) as a conjunctive particle in oaths, where depending on whether it acts as a substitute for the verb (na'ib 'an al-'amil) or simply links, breaking the oath may require committing all acts or just any one; this leads to a contextual determination because Shariah prohibits each offense individually. (2) For permissibility (ibaha) versus choice (takhyir): the key distinction is that ibaha allows gathering all options, while takhyir forces a choice of only one. The example 'sit with the fuqaha or the muhaddithin' illustrates ibaha because one may sit with both. The rule is that an exception after a prohibition indicates ibaha, not choice. (3) Metaphorical use as 'hatta' (until) when a accusative present tense verb follows 'aw' and no accusative present tense precedes; then 'aw' indicates the endpoint of a continuous state, e.g., 'I will certainly stay with you or you give me my right' means 'until you give me my right'. (4) Application to the Qur'anic verse 'ليس لك من الأمر شيء أو يتوب عليهم أو يعذبهم' (Qur'an 3:128): an objection arises that the verse seems to end the Prophet's involvement in the matter when either mercy or punishment occurs, yet the matter is always Allah's. Two resolutions are offered: first, the Prophet's distress (from being wounded at Uhud) is removed by Allah's turning or punishing; second, the prohibition of cursing was temporary, and 'aw' means 'until', so the matter ends with either outcome but remains Allah's. Finally, an oath example: 'I will not enter this house or I enter that house' – since 'aw' is followed by an accusative present verb without a preceding accusative, conjunction is impossible; the oath means the non-entry of the first lasts until entry of the second, so entering the first directly breaks the oath.

Key Terms
اباحة (ibaha): Permissibility: allows combining all options (e.g., sitting with both fuqaha and muhaddithin). Opposite of takhyir which restricts to one.
تخيير (takhyir): Choice: allows only one option, preventing combination of all.
حتى (hatta): Until; a particle indicating the limit of a continuous state. 'Aw' is metaphorically used in this sense under specific grammatical conditions.

Checkpoint
In the statement 'I will not speak to anyone except Zaid or Amr', what is the legal effect of 'aw' according to the Hanafi rule from this lesson?
A) He may speak to only one of them.
B) He may speak to both without breaking the oath.
C) He must speak to both or the oath is broken.
D) The oath is invalid because of the exception.

Key Takeaway
Exception after prohibition makes 'aw' indicate permissibility (ibaha), and 'aw' can metaphorically mean 'until' (hatta) when followed by a mansub mudari' without a preceding mansub mudari'.

The Three Meanings of Hatta in Legal Contexts
Analysing the three possible meanings of hatta with examples from oaths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three possible meanings of hatta and apply the Hanafi criteria to determine which meaning is intended in a given legal text.

Core Idea
The particle hatta can indicate an end, a cause, or mere conjunction, depending on the nature of the linked actions.

The particle hatta is common in oaths and legal statements, but its meaning is not fixed. Hanafi usul provides clear criteria to decide whether it indicates an endpoint (ghayah), a cause (sababiyyah), or mere conjunction (atf). Understanding this classification is essential for correctly interpreting legal obligations and oath-breaking.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two actions
Determine what is before hatta (sadr) and after hatta (akhir). These are usually actions or events.
In oaths, the actions are often conditional and outcome.
2
Check extensibility of the first action
Ask: can the first action reasonably extend over time? If it is instantaneous, it cannot be extended.
Fighting, beating, prohibiting entry are extensible; embracing Islam at a moment is not if taken as onset.
3
Check if the second action can be an end
Ask: can the second action serve as a natural termination point of the first action? If yes, hatta is ghayah.
Screaming ends beating; paying jizyah ends fighting; seeking permission ends prohibition.
4
If not ghayah, check causality
If the first action can be the cause of the second, then hatta is sababiyyah (so that).
Coming to be fed: coming causes feeding.
5
If neither, it is mere conjunction (atf)
If both extensibility/end and causality fail, hatta indicates mere atf, joining two actions without implying sequence or cause.
This is a juristic extension, not found in classical Arabic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اور کبھی صرف سبیہ کے لئے ہوتا ہے اور کبھی برائے عطف یعنی محض تشریک معطوف مع المعطوف علیہ کرے گا من غیر اعتبارہ غایتہ و سیبیہ - فان احتمل یہ ان اقسام ثلثہ کی تفصیل ہے یعنی ان احتمل الصدرالامتداد یعنی کیونکہ صدر کلام یعنی ضرب محتمل امتداد ہے اور حتی تصیح صلاحیت برائے انتہا رکھتا ہے کیونکہ صباح مضروب عموماً ضرب کے لئے منتہی بن جاتا ہے تو اس مثال میں حقی برائے عایشہ ہے اگر اس نے صباح مضروب سے پہلے اپنی ضرب روک لی تو وہ حانث ہو جائے گا اور بعد ازار ہو جائیگا۔ اگر یہاں تک مارتا رہا کہ مضروب نے چیخ پکار کی تو پھر حانث نہیں ہوگا۔ وان قال عبدی حران لم انك حتى تفدینی اس مثال میں حتی برائے سببیہ ہے برائے غایتہ نہیں ہے کیونکہ آخر کلام یعنی تفریہ ماقبل کے لئے یعنی انسان کے لئے انتہامہ بننے کی صلاحیت نہیں رکھتا بلکہ یہ داعی الی الاتیان ہے اور اتیان صلاحیت سببیہ رکھتا ہے اور غدا جز ابن سکتی ہے لہذا عندک یعنی وہ کہتا ہے ان لم اتک حتی القدس عندک تو اس مثال میں حتی صرف برائے عطف ہے کیونکہ غایتہ اور سبیتہ دونوں متعذر ہیں غایتہ نہیں ہو سکتی اور اس کی وجہ گزر چکی ہے اور سببیہ نہی نہیں ہو سکتی کیونکہ ایک اور اتغدی دونوں فعل متکلم ہیں اور ایک ہی شخص کا اپنا فعل اپنے فعل کی جزا نہیں بن سکتا لہذا یہاں سبیہ اور مجازاۃ کا مفہوم نہیں ہو سکتا لہذا یہاں عطف محض ہو گا ۔ فصار اب یہ مثال حتی والی ایسے ہو جائے گی جیسے عطف بالفاء ہوتا ہے کقولہ ان لم اتك فاتغدى عندك اگراس کے پاس آگرہ بلا تراخی غذا کھائی تو تیمین اس کی پوری ہو جائے گی حانث نہیں ہوگا اور حتی کا عطف محض کے لئے ہونا اس کی نظیر کلام عرب میں موجود نہیں ہے بلکہ فقہا نے اس کا اختراع کیا ہے بطور استعارہ اور مجار کے۔
And sometimes it is only for sababiyyah, and sometimes for atf, i.e., it will purely share the ma'tuf with the ma'tuf 'alayh without considering it as ghayah or sababiyyah. So if it is possible, this is the detail of the three divisions: if the first part (sadr) can extend, i.e., what is before hatta can possibly extend, and the last part (akhir) has the capacity to be the end, i.e., the first part extends and ends at the last part and has the capacity to be cut off, then hatta in such a place will be for ghayah, like 'until they pay the jizyah' – what is before hatta, i.e., fighting, can extend, and accepting jizyah has the capacity to be its end. Similarly, 'until you seek permission' – here what is before hatta is the prohibition of entering another's house, which can extend, and seeking permission has the capacity to be its end. If the first part does not have the possibility of extension and the last part does not have the capacity to be the end, then it will be seen: if the first part can be a cause for the second part, i.e., if the first part has the capacity to be a cause for the action that comes after hatta, then this hatta is in the meaning of 'because' which indicates sababiyyah, because the reward and the caused are usually intended from the thing, so they are like the end. For example, 'I embraced Islam so that I enter Paradise' – here the meaning of ghayah is not possible because if by 'I embraced Islam' is meant the inception of Islam, then there is no extension; if is meant steadfastness on Islam, then entering Paradise does not have the capacity to be its end and point of termination, rather with entering Paradise, Islam becomes more firm and established. Therefore, here hatta is for sababiyyah. As for atf, if the first part (what is before hatta) cannot be a cause for the second (what is after hatta), then that hatta will be for mere atf, not for ghayah nor for sababiyyah. And in mere atf, the existence of two actions is a condition so that sharing is realized, which is required by atf. For example, if someone says, 'My slave is free if I do not beat you until you scream' – this explanation is like the above. Now this example is of ghayah: hatta here is for ghayah because the first part of the statement, i.e., beating, can extend, and 'until you scream' has the capacity to be the end, because the one being beaten usually becomes the end of the beating. So in this example, hatta is for ghayah. If he stops his beating before the beating results in screaming, he will be a hanith (oath-breaker); if he continues beating until the beaten one screams, then he will not be a hanith. And if he says, 'My slave is free if I do not come to you until you feed me' – in this example, hatta is for sababiyyah, not for ghayah, because the last part, i.e., 'you feed me' does not have the capacity to be the end for what is before, i.e., for the person, but rather it is a motivator for coming, and coming has the capacity to be sababiyyah, and food is a part of it, so it is carried on sababiyyah. So if he came and the addressee did not feed him, he is not a hanith. And if he says, 'until the food with you' i.e., he says, 'If I do not come to you until I eat food with you' – in this example, hatta is merely for atf, because both ghayah and sababiyyah are impossible. Ghayah is not possible for the reason already mentioned, and sababiyyah is not possible because coming and eating are both actions of the speaker, and one's own action cannot be the cause of another action of oneself. Therefore, here sababiyyah and compensation are not conceivable, so here it is mere atf. So now this example with hatta becomes like atf with fa', as in his saying, 'If I do not come to you, then I eat food with you' – if he came and without delay ate food, his oath is fulfilled and he is not a hanith. And the use of hatta for mere atf has no precedent in Arabic speech; rather the jurists invented it metaphorically and figuratively.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analysis of the particle 'hatta' in legal contexts, specifically oaths. The author explains the three possible meanings: ghayah (extent/end), sababiyyah (cause), and atf (mere conjunction). The classification depends on whether the first part of the sentence can extend and whether the second part can be its natural end. If both hold, hatta is ghayah. If only the first can be a cause for the second, it is sababiyyah. If neither, it is mere atf. Examples from oaths illustrate each: beating until screaming (ghayah), coming to be fed (sababiyyah), and coming to eat together (atf). The last use is deemed a juristic invention metaphorically.

Key Terms
حتى (hattā): A particle that can indicate extent, cause, or mere conjunction depending on context.
غاية (ghāyah): The meaning of 'until' indicating the end point of an action that can extend.
سببية (sababiyyah): The meaning of 'so that' indicating that the first action is the cause of the second.
عطف (ʿaṭf): Mere conjunction without implying end or cause, used where both actions are from the same agent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the oath 'My slave is free if I do not beat you until you scream,' what does 'hatta' indicate?
A) Ghayah (end)
B) Sababiyyah (cause)
C) Atf (conjunction)
D) Takhsis (specification)

Key Takeaway
The function of hatta in legal texts is determined by the nature of the linked actions: ghayah requires extensibility, sababiyyah requires causality, and atf applies when neither holds.

Meanings of the Particle Bā' in Legal Contexts
Explaining the meanings of baa: ilṣāq, istiʿānah, etc., with examples from oaths and contracts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the meanings of bā' (ilṣāq and istiʿānah) and apply the correct meaning to interpret legal texts such as sales contracts and oaths.

Core Idea
The particle bā' changes the legal ruling: in 'I wrote with the pen' it shows means; in 'with my permission' it shows attachment.

In this lesson, we explore the dual meanings of the Arabic particle bā' and how they affect legal interpretation. The Hanafi usul tradition carefully distinguishes between bā' for istiʿānah (instrumentality) and bā' for ilṣāq (attachment). Misidentifying the meaning can lead to incorrect rulings in sales and oaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary meanings of bā'
Learn the two core meanings: ilṣāq (attachment) and istiʿānah (seeking help/using a means). The passage defines these and gives examples.
Ilṣāq connects the following noun to the verb as an attached condition; istiʿānah indicates the instrument or means.
2
Apply bā' of istiʿānah to sales contracts
Analyze the examples: 'I sold this slave for wheat' vs 'I sold wheat for the slave.' The bā' on the price (wheat or slave) shows it is a means, determining whether the contract is an ordinary sale or a salam sale.
If the bā' enters on the price (thaman), the price is a means; the rules of substitution and possession differ accordingly.
3
Apply bā' of ilṣāq to oaths
Analyze the oath 'Do not go out except with my permission.' The bā' in 'bi-idhnī' indicates ilṣāq, meaning the permission is attached to the act of going out. Thus every exit without permission violates the oath.
The exception 'except with my permission' is analyzed as an istithnā' mufarraq, where the mustathnā minhu is implicit.
4
Synthesize the two meanings
Compare the legal consequences: in sales, bā' of istiʿānah determines the nature of the contract; in oaths, bā' of ilṣāq makes permission a condition of validity.
Understanding the correct meaning is crucial for issuing correct fatwas in such cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(حروف الجر) الباء للإنصاف الحاق کا معنی ہے اتصال الشي بالشي والاستعانه ای طلب المعونته بشتی علی شنی یعنی ایک شتی کے ساتھ دوسری شتی پر مدد طلب کرنا جیسے کتبت بالقلم تو قلم معین علی الکتابتہ ہے ۔ فتدخل على الوسائل یہ تفریح على الاستعانتہ ہے ۔ چونکہ وسائل کے ساتھ استعانت علی المقاصد ہوتی ہے اس لئے بارہ استعانتہ وسائل پر داخل ہوتی ہے کا لاشمان جیسے بیوی میں اثمان وسائل ہیں اور مقصود اصلی مبیع ہے تاکہ انتفاع بالمبیع المملوک حاصل ہو سکے اور اشمان جو عموما نقود سے ہوتے ہیں یہ مقصود بالذات نہیں ہوتے بلکہ یہ بمنزلہ آلات کے ہیں جن سے توسل الی المقاصد ہوتا ہے ۔ فان قال بعت هذا العبد بکر تو اس صورت میں عبد مبیع ہوگا اور گر جنط شمن ہوگا جو فی الحال ثابت فی الذمہ ہو جائیگا۔ دنی بعت گرم بالعبد تو اس صورت میں بیع سلم ہو جائیگی اور عبد راس المال ہوگا۔ اور کر حفطہ مسلم فیہ ہوگا۔ خیر اعلی شرائطہ اور اس ثانی صورت میں بیع سلم کے شرائط کی رعایت کی جائیگی مثلا تا جیل شرط ہوگی اور راس المال کا قبض فی المجلس شرط ہوگا ولا یجری الاستبدال فی الکر یعنی کر حنطہ کے بدلہ میں کوئی اور شئی لے لینا یہ قبل قبض الکر جائز نہیں ہوگا بخلاف الدول بخلاف صورۃ اولی کے چونکہ اس اس میں کر ثمن ہے لہذا استبدال في الكر جائز ہوگا جیسے اثمان میں ہوتا ہے خان قال لا تخرج الا باذنی یه تفريع على الالصاق ہے اس صورۃ میں ہر خروج کے لئے اذن واجب ہوگی کیونکہ اس کا مفہوم ہے الا خروجا ملصقا بازنی وجہ اس کی یہ ہے کہ یہ استثناء مفرع ہے اور یہاں مستثنی منہ مقدر ہے اور وہ ہوگا عام اور ماقبل کی جنس سے ہو گا معنی یوں ہو گا لا تخرج خروجاً الآخروجاً ملصقا بازنی اور خروج جو مستثنی منہ ہے وہ نکرہ تحت النفی ہے فائدہ شوم کا دیگا اور اس سے صرف خروج ملصق بالاذن کا استثناء کیا گیا ہے اس کے علاوہ باقی تہ ام خروج حکم نفی میں درج رہیں گے اور جو خروج منلصق بالاذن
(Particles of Jar) The baa is for ilṣāq (attachment) meaning connection of one thing with another, and istiʿānah (seeking help) i.e., seeking assistance by means of one thing upon another, like 'I wrote with the pen' – the pen is an aid for writing. So it enters upon means (wasā'il). That is a branch of istiʿānah. Since seeking help with means to achieve ends, baa istiʿānah enters upon means, like prices (athmān) in a sale – prices are means, and the main purpose is the sold item, so that benefit from the owned sold item can be obtained. And prices which are usually from currencies are not intended for themselves but are like tools through which one reaches purposes. So if he says, 'I sold this slave for wheat,' then in that case the slave is the sold item and the wheat is the price which becomes established in the dhimmah immediately. And if he says, 'I sold wheat for the slave,' then in that case it becomes a salam sale, and the slave is the capital (ra's al-māl), and the wheat is the muslam fīhi (subject matter). Subject to its conditions. And in this second case, the conditions of salam sale are observed, e.g., deferment is a condition, and possession of the capital in the session is a condition. And substitution in the wheat is not permissible, i.e., taking something else in place of wheat before possessing the wheat is not permissible, unlike the first case, because in the first case the wheat is the price, so substitution in the wheat is permissible, as occurs with prices. For example, if he says, 'Do not go out except with my permission' – this is a branch of ilṣāq. In this case, permission is obligatory for every going out, because its meaning is 'no going out except a going out attached to my permission.' The reason is that this is an exceptional sentence and the istithnā' is mufarraq, and the mustathnā minhu is implied, and it is general and of the same genus as what precedes. The meaning becomes: 'Do not go out with any going out except a going out attached to my permission.' And the going out which is the mustathnā minhu is a nakirah under negation, so it gives generality. And only the going out attached to permission is excepted; the remaining types of going out remain under the negation. And the going out attached to permission...
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage discusses the particle baa and its various meanings in legal contexts. Baa can indicate ilṣāq (attachment) as in 'do not go out except with my permission,' where the exception implies attachment. It can also indicate istiʿānah (seeking help) as in 'I wrote with the pen.' The author applies this to contracts: if one says 'I sold this slave for wheat,' the wheat is the price (thaman) and the slave is the sold item (mabīʿ). If reversed, 'I sold wheat for the slave,' it becomes a salam contract where the slave is the capital and wheat is the subject matter. Different rules apply: in salam, the capital must be possessed in the session and substitution before possession is not allowed. The passage also analyzes oaths like 'do not go out except with my permission,' concluding that any exit without permission violates the oath.

Key Terms
الباء (al-bāʾ): A particle of jar with primary meanings of ilṣāq (attachment) and istiʿānah (seeking help).
إلصاق (ilṣāq): Attachment or joining, as in an exception that attaches the permission to the action.
استعانة (istiʿānah): Seeking help or using a means, as in writing with a pen.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person swears 'I will not go out except with my permission,' what does the baa in 'bā'idhnī' indicate?
A) Istiʿānah (seeking help)
B) Ilṣāq (attachment)
C) Sababiyyah (cause)
D) Takhsis (specification)

Key Takeaway
The particle bā' in legal texts must be interpreted according to context: in instrumental phrases it indicates istiʿānah, in conditional oaths it indicates ilṣāq (attachment).

The Function of Hatta in Hanafi Oaths: From Purpose to Mere Conjunction
Textual analysis of Hatta in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul rule that Hatta in oaths indicates mere conjunction ('atf mahd) and explain why its literal meanings are inapplicable.

Core Idea
Hatta in oaths: mere conjunction, not purpose or cause, because both actions belong to the same agent.

This lesson explores a distinctive Hanafi interpretation of the letter Hatta in oath formulations. In classical Arabic, Hatta typically signals a purpose (ghāyah) or cause (sababiyyah). However, Hanafi jurists argue that when the same person performs both actions in an oath, those meanings become impossible. Instead, Hatta functions as mere conjunction ('atf mahd), like Fa, requiring immediate succession. This is a metaphorical extension invented by jurists, not a literal usage found in Arabic speech.

Guided Path
1
Identify the letter Hatta in the oath
Locate Hatta in the example oath 'in lam atka hatta ataghadda 'indaka'. Note that it connects two actions of the same subject.
The oath has a conditional structure: 'if I do not come... then I will eat...'.
2
List the literal meanings of Hatta
Recall that Hatta can indicate purpose (ghāyah) or cause (sababiyyah). Understand why these are problematic when the agent is identical.
Ghāyah: 'until I eat' implies purpose. Sababiyyah: 'so that I eat' implies cause.
3
Determine why literal meanings are impossible
Explain that purpose or cause requires a different agent or a natural sequence. Here, the speaker's own actions cannot cause themselves as purpose; the oath links two events by the same person.
For example, 'I came so that I would eat' has two different actions but same subject, yet one is not the cause of the other in a conditional oath.
4
Apply the Hanafi rule of 'atf mahd
Conclude that Hatta is interpreted as mere conjunction, similar to Fa, requiring immediate action. This prevents the oath from being broken by delay.
The ruling: if he eats without delay after the condition is triggered, the oath is fulfilled.
5
Note the juristic invention
Acknowledge that this usage is not found in classical Arabic but is a constructed metaphorical extension by Hanafi jurists.
This is a valid istihsan-like reasoning to achieve legal objectives.

فصار اب یہ مثال حتی والی ایسے ہو جائے گی جیسے عطف بالفاء ہوتا ہے کقولہ ان لم اتك فاتغدى عندك اگراس کے پاس آگرہ بلا تراخی غذا کھائی تو تیمین اس کی پوری ہو جائے گی حانث نہیں ہوگا اور حتی کا عطف محض کے لئے ہونا اس کی نظیر کلام عرب میں موجود نہیں ہے بلکہ فقہا نے اس کا اختراع کیا ہے بطور استعارہ اور مجار کے۔
So now this example of Hatta becomes like conjunction with Fa, like his saying: 'If I do not come to you, I will eat food at your place without delay.' Then his oath will be fulfilled, he will not be sinful. And Hatta being used for mere conjunction has no precedent in Arabic speech; rather, jurists invented it by way of metaphor and figurative usage.

Commentary
This passage analyzes the letter Hatta in the context of oaths. The author (from a Hanafi usul commentary) explains that Hatta sometimes cannot carry its original meanings of purpose (ghāyah) or cause (sababiyyah) because both are impossible here—the speaker's own action cannot be the cause of itself. Thus jurists treat Hatta as mere conjunction ('atf mahd), similar to Fa, requiring immediate action. The author notes this usage is not found in classical Arabic but was invented by jurists as a metaphorical extension. This is a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding interpretation of oaths.

Key Terms
حتى (hatta): A letter that can indicate purpose (ghāyah) or cause (sababiyyah), but here interpreted as mere conjunction ('atf mahd).
عطف محض (atf mahd): Mere conjunction without additional meaning of cause or purpose.

A) Because Hatta in Arabic never indicates purpose.
B) Because the two actions are by the same person, making purpose or cause impossible.
C) Because jurists arbitrarily changed its meaning.
D) Because Hatta indicates immediate succession.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, Hatta in oaths is interpreted as mere conjunction ('atf mahd) when the same agent performs both actions, requiring immediate performance to fulfill the oath.

Effect of 'با' on Wiping: Tool vs. Locus
Explaining the usul rule of 'با' in wiping and its exception in tayammum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle of the particle 'با' to determine the extent of wiping required when it enters on a tool versus a locus.

Core Idea
When 'با' enters on a tool, only a part suffices; on a locus, only a part is needed unless overridden.

This lesson unpacks a subtle Hanafi usul rule regarding the Arabic preposition 'با' in the context of wiping (مسح). The grammatical entry of 'با' changes whether the noun is treated as a mere tool or as the object of the action, thereby affecting the obligation of completion. We'll see why wiping the head in wudu requires only a quarter, while wiping the face in tayammum requires the full face despite the same verse construction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the grammatical construction
Determine whether 'با' precedes a tool (e.g., 'بيد') or a locus (e.g., 'برؤوس').
The tool is the instrument; the locus is the object being wiped.
2
Apply the core rule
If 'با' enters on a tool, only an amount sufficient to transfer the effect is needed; completion of the locus is required. If on a locus, that locus is likened to a tool, so only a part of it must be wiped.
Example: 'مسحت الحائط بيدي' → tool (يد) → only part of the hand suffices, but the entire wall must be wiped.
3
Check for overriding evidence
Even if 'با' enters on the locus, the default partial ruling can be overridden by principles like substitution (خلف) or explicit hadith.
In tayammum, 'بوجوهكم' has 'با' on the locus, but completion is required because wiping is a substitute for washing (which requires completion), and the hadith of 'Ammar explicitly says two strikes cover the whole face.
4
Derive the specific fiqh ruling
From the rule and overrides, derive: wiping the head in wudu requires one-quarter (forelock); wiping the face in tayammum requires the entire face.
The hadith of the forelock (حديث الناصية) specifies the quarter for the head.

وقالوا ان دخلت فی الة المسلم یعنی اگر با بر آلہ مسح پر داخل ہو تو چونکہ آلہ میں صرف اتنا مقدار معتبر ہوتا ہے جس کے ساتھ مقصود حاصل ہو جائے استیعاب في الالہ شرط نہیں کیونکہ آلہ مقصود بالذات نہیں ہوتا وہ صرف واسطہ بین الفاعل والمنفعل ہوتا ہے تاکہ اس کے ذریعہ سے اثر فاعل منفعل تک پہونچے جائے اور اس وقت مقصود فی الفعل المتعدی صرف محل ہو گا لہذا استيعاب الہ واجب نہیں ہو گا بلکہ اس سے اتنا مقدار کافی ہو گی جس کے ساتھ مقصود حاصل ہو جائے البتہ اس صورت میں استیعاب محل ضروری ہوگا۔ نحو مسحت الحائط بید کی تو یہاں باید پر داخل ہے جو آلہ مسیح ہے يتعدى الى المحل تو فعل مسح بواسطه با کے متعدی الی المحل یعنی فخلاف یہ کے کہ اس کے اندر کل مراد نہیں بلکہ صرف اتنا مقداد مراد ہوگی جس کے ساتھ مسح ہو سکے وہ ہے مس بباطن الكف فان دخلت فی المحل اگر لفظ بار محل پر داخل ہو جیسے را مسحوا بوجوہکم میں کہ راس محل مسح ہے۔ لا یتناول كل المحل تو یہ مسیح کل محل کو متناول اور شامل بو نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ اس وقت محل شبیه بالالہ ہو جائیں گے چونکہ یہ حرف بار مخصوص بالالہ ہے اور داخل على المحل ہے تو لا محالہ یہاں محل کو تشبیه بالاتر ہوگی فلا یراد کله توکل محل مراد نہیں بلکہ بعض حصہ محل کا یعنی راس کامراد ہوگا تقديره الصقوها بروسكم اى الصقوا المسح بروسکم تو مراد ہو گا بعض الراس اور اس بعض کو حدیث ناصیہ نے بیان فرما دیا کہ بقدر ناصیہ یعنی ربع رآس کا مسح فرض ہے ۔ وانما يثبت استعیاب الوجه في التیمی المیہ ایک اشکال کا جواب ہے۔ اشکال یہ ہے کہ آیتہ تیم میں ہے وامسحوا بوجوهكم اور باداخل علی المحل ہے تو پھر حسب ضابطہ وجہ کا استیعاب بالمسح تیم میں واجب نہ ہو حالانکہ استیعاب وجہ بالاتفاق واجب ہے تو اس کے دو جواب دیتے اول لان المسح خلف عن الغسل یعنی یہ مسح وجہ خلف ہے غسل وجہ کا جو وضو میں ہوتا تھا چونکہ غسل وجہ میں استیعاب مراد ہو سکتا تھا لیکن حدیث مشہور نے کل وجہ کا مسح واجب قرار دیا اس کے ساتھ کتاب اللہ پر زیادہ صحیح ہے۔
And they said: if it enters upon the tool of the made object, i.e., if 'ba' enters upon the tool of wiping, then since in the tool only that amount is considered sufficient with which the purpose is achieved, completion in the tool is not a condition because the tool is not intended for itself; it is only a means between the doer and the object, so that through it the effect of the doer reaches the object. And at that time the intended thing in the transitive verb is only the locus, therefore completion of the tool is not obligatory, rather that amount from it is sufficient which achieves the purpose. However, in this case completion of the locus is necessary. For example, 'masahtu al-hā'ita bi-yadi' – here 'ba' enters upon 'yad' which is the tool of wiping, and it becomes transitive to the locus, so the verb of wiping via 'ba' becomes transitive to the locus, i.e., 'ila al-hā'it', so it includes all of it, i.e., wiping includes the entire wall, because 'al-hā'it' is a name for the whole, and it is mentioned as intended for itself, so it is intended that all of the wall is intended. Contrary to this, that not all is intended, rather only that amount is intended with which wiping can be done, which is wiping with the inner side of the palm. If it enters upon the locus, like 'imsahū bi-ru'ūsikum' where the head is the locus of wiping, it does not include all of the locus. So this wiping does not encompass and include the entire locus, because at that time the locus becomes similar to the tool. Since this letter 'ba' is specific to the tool and enters upon the locus, necessarily here the locus is likened to the tool, so not all of it is intended, rather some part of the locus, i.e., part of the head, is intended. Its interpretation is 'al-siqūhā bi-ru'ūsikum', i.e., attach the wiping to your heads. So part of the head is intended, and that part is expressed by the hadith of the forelock: that wiping the amount of the forelock, i.e., a quarter of the head, is obligatory. And the establishment of completion of the face in tayammum is an answer to an objection. The objection is that in the verse of tayammum it says 'wa-msahū bi-wujūhikum', and 'ba' enters upon the locus. So according to the rule, completion of the face in wiping in tayammum would not be obligatory, whereas completion of the face is unanimously obligatory. So they give two answers: first, because this wiping is a substitute for washing, i.e., this wiping of the face is a substitute for the washing of the face that occurs in wudu, and since completion is obligatory in washing, and wiping in tayammum is its substitute and representative, therefore completion is obligatory in it as well. Or by the hadith of 'Ammar, which is the second answer: i.e., it is conceded that according to the rule in tayammum part of the face would be intended, but since in the hadith of 'Ammar it is mentioned: 'Innamā yakfīka darbatāni: darbatun lil-wajhi wa-darbatun lil-dhirā'ayn', so in this hadith the whole face is intended, and this is a well-known hadith, so it is permissible to add upon the meaning of the Book. So from the Book, part of the face could be intended, but the well-known hadith made wiping the whole face obligatory, and adding upon the Book by it is valid.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage explains a key usul principle regarding the particle 'با' when it enters upon a noun in the context of wiping (masah). The distinction is made between two cases: when 'با' enters upon the tool (آلة) and when it enters upon the locus (محل). When 'با' enters upon the tool, only a portion of the tool is needed because the tool is a means. Example: 'مَسَحْتُ الْحَائِطَ بِيَدِي' – here 'با' is on 'يَد' (tool), so wiping the entire wall is required because the wall is the locus and intended. But when 'با' enters upon the locus, as in 'امْسَحُوا بِرُؤُوسِكُمْ' – here 'با' is on 'رُؤُوس' (locus), then only a part of the head is required to be wiped, because the locus is treated like a tool due to the entry of 'با'. The Hanafi ruling based on this is that one-quarter of the head (the forelock) suffices for wiping in wudu. The passage then addresses an objection regarding tayammum: the verse 'وَامْسَحُوا بِوُجُوهِكُمْ' has 'با' on 'وُجُوه' (locus), so by the same rule, only part of the face would be required, but it is obligatory to wipe the entire face in tayammum. Two answers are given: (1) wiping in tayammum is a substitute for washing (ghusl) which requires completion, so it inherits that rule; (2) the well-known hadith of 'Ammar explicitly states two strikes – one for the face and one for the arms – indicating the whole face must be wiped, and such a mashhur hadith can add to the general implication of the Qur'an. This shows the Hanafi method of harmonizing Qur'anic generalities with prophetic clarification.

Key Terms
آلة (āla): The tool or instrument by which an action is performed (e.g., hand in wiping).
محل (mahall): The locus or object of the action (e.g., head, wall).
مسح خلف عن الغسل (masḥ khalaf 'an al-ghusl): Wiping in tayammum is a substitute for washing in wudu, inheriting its rules of completion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The entire tool must be used; the locus must be fully wiped.
B) Only a sufficient part of the tool is needed; the locus must be fully wiped.
C) The entire tool must be used; only a part of the locus is needed.
D) Only a sufficient part of the tool is needed; only a part of the locus is needed.

Key Takeaway
The entry of 'با' in a wiping verb triggers a Hanafi usul rule: on the tool, only a part suffices; on the locus, only a part is needed unless overridden by substitution or explicit hadith.

The Four Grammatical Madhhabs on 'Ila' and the Hanafi Resolution
Dispute over the interpretation of 'ila' and Hanafi resolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the four grammatical madhhabs on the particle 'ila', explain the Hanafi chosen view (fourth madhhab), and apply it to the rulings of wudu and fasting.

Core Idea
The Hanafi fourth madhhab resolves the ambiguity of 'ila': inclusion depends on semantic encompassment, not arbitrary rule.

The particle 'ila' (to/until) is central to many Quranic and legal texts. Does it include or exclude the endpoint? The grammarians differ, leading to contradictory fiqh rulings. This lesson unpacks the four views and shows why Hanafi usul adopts a conditional approach that yields certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios of ghayah
Determine whether the ghayah (endpoint) is a real endpoint in reality before the speaker's utterance, or becomes a ghayah only by the speaker's expression. The default ruling differs.
Examples: 'I ate the fish up to its head' (real endpoint) vs. 'wash your hands up to the elbows' (speech-induced ghayah).
2
Outline the four grammatical madhhabs
List the four positions: (1) inclusion is real, exclusion is metaphorical; (2) exclusion is real, inclusion is metaphorical; (3) literal ambiguity; (4) inclusion if the ghayah is of the same genus as the maghya (or the maghya term encompasses the ghayah).
The first two are contradictory; the third introduces doubt.
3
Understand the Hanafi fourth madhhab's condition
The condition is linguistic encompassment: if the term of the maghya (e.g., 'yad' meaning hand) naturally includes the ghayah (elbow), then the ghayah is included in the ruling. If not (e.g., 'sawm' does not include night), it is excluded.
This is also expressed by grammarians as 'if the ghayah is of the same genus as the maghya'.
4
Apply to the wudu verse (5:6) and fasting verse (2:187)
In wudu: 'wash your hands up to the elbows' – 'yad' includes the elbow, so elbows are washed. In fasting: 'complete the fast until night' – 'sawm' does not include night, so night is not part of the fast.
These applications show consistent outcomes.
5
Evaluate why the fourth madhhab is preferred
The first two madhhabs are contradictory (mutawarid), causing doubt. The third (ambiguity) also causes doubt. The fourth resolves doubt by specifying a condition, ensuring the default ruling (certainty) is not overturned by doubt.
The principle: 'Certainty is not removed by doubt.' The fourth madhhab acts as the result of the three schools.

Source Text

غایت تک پہونچائیگی اور فایته خارج از حکم رہیگی نحوا تموا الصيام الی اللیل تو صیام لیل کو متناول نہیں ہے لہذا یہ حکم قیام میں داخل نہیں ہوگی فیحرم الوصال فی الصیام و ان تناول اگر صدر کلام اس غانیہ کو متناول ہے جیسے اید یکم الی المرافق میں لفظ یہ مرفق کو متناول ہے کیونکہ یہ کا اطلاق اصابع سے لیکر مناکب واباط تک ہوتا ہے نذكرها لاسقاط ما ورائها اس وقت ذکر غایہ ماوراء غایتہ کے اسقاط کے لئے ہو گا الی المرافق کا لفظ حکم غسل کو مناکب سے لیکر مرافق تک اسقاط عمل کر دے گا اور مرافق فاغسلوا اید یکم کے حکم میں داخل رہیں گی فتدخل تحت المغیا اسی تحت حكم المغيا وهو الغسل وللنحويين في الى اربعة مذاهب الی کے متعلق جو مصنف کے نزدیک مختار تھا اس کی تفصیل کر دی یہاں سے اپنے مختار پر دلیل پیش کر رہے ہیں حاصل یہ کہ الی کے متعلق نحویوں کے مذاہب اربعہ ہیں۔ پہلا مذہب ہے۔ الدخول الامجازا یعنی غایتہ حکم مخیا کے تحت داخل ہوگی یہ الی کا حقیقی معنی ہے اور عدم دخول مجاز ہے دعکسہ کا یہ مذہب ثانی ہے الی کا حقیقی مفہوم یہ ہے کہ غایتہ حکم مفیا کے تحت داخل نہیں ہوگی اور دخول فی حکم المغیا یہ مجاز ہے کا لمرافق تو اس مذہب ثانی کے لحاظ دخول مرافق تحت حكم المغياء هو الغسل مجازاً ہوگا حقیقت نہیں. والاشتراک یہ مذہب ثالث ہے یعنی الی دونوں معنی دخول اور عدم دخول میں مشترک ہے اس مذہب ثالث کے لحاظ سے دخول غايته تحت حكم المغیا اور عدم دخول دونوں حقیقی معنی ہوں گے کیونکہ اشتراک میں دونوں معنی موضوع نہ ہوتے ہیں والدخول ان كان ما بعدها من جنس ما قبلها به مذهب رابع ہے حاصل یہ کہ اگر ما بعد الی ماقبل کی جنس سے سے تو ما بعد محکم ما قبل میں داخل ہوگا اگر جنس ما قبل سے نہیں ہے تو پھر داخل نہیں ہوگا وما ذکر نانی اللیل ہم نے جولیل کے متعلق کہا کہ صدر کلام چونکہ اس کو متناول نہیں ہے لہذا الیل تحت حکم المغیا داخل نہیں ہوگی اور مرافق میں چونکہ صدر کلام اس غایتہ کو متناول ہے لہذا مرافق تحت حکم المنیا داخل ہوں گی یہ اسی مذہب رابع کے مناسب ہے۔ معنی ما ذکرنا یعنی جو کچھ ہم نے ذکر کیا اور جو کی نحویوں نے ذکر کیا منی متحد ہیں صرف اختلاف عبارت اور اختلاف الفاظ ہے کیونکہ نحویوں نے جو کہا ہے کہ غایتہ اگر جنس معیا سے ہو تو اس کا معنی یہی ہے کہ اگر لفظ مغیا فایتہ کو متناول ہو تو یہ صرف تعبیری اور لفظی فرق اور معنوی اتحاد ہے انما ایک جانب یقینی نہیں ہو سکتی یہ موجب شک ہیں اور مذہب ثالث اشتراک والا یہ بھی موجب شک ہے کیونکہ اشتراک میں کوئی ایک جانب قطعی اور یقینی نہیں ہو سکتی پس فیصلہ یہی ہے که اگر صد سر کلام متناول للعایتہ نہیں تو اس کا دخول تحت حکم المغیا شک کی بنا پر نہیں ہو سکتا اگر صد کام اسے متناول ہے تو اب اس کا خروج شک کے ساتھ نہیں ہوسکتا بہر حال مذاہب ثلثہ موجب شک ہیں اور شک کی بنا پر حکم سابق تبدیل نہیں ہو سکتا۔ وبعض الشارحين قالوا هي غاية الاسقاط الم بعض شارحین متاخرین نے متقدمین کی کلام کی تشریح اور توجیہہ بایں طور بیان کی ہے کہ الی برائے غایتہ ہے والغاية لا تدخل الخ اور غایتہ مفیا کے تحت مطلقا داخل نہیں ہے Whether or not, according to the author, the chosen view, he detailed it saying: 'then the ghayah...'. Before the speaker speaks, if that ghayah is actually a ghayah in reality—i.e., before the speaker used the word 'ila' to make it a ghayah—it was in fact a ghayah in real existence, it will not enter the ruling of what precedes it, like 'I sent this garden from this wall to that wall' and 'I ate the fish up to its head'—the ghayah does not enter under the maghya. He pointed to other examples: whether the beginning of the speech includes the ghayah, like 'fish' and 'head', or does not include it, like 'garden' and 'wall', in both cases the ghayah does not enter the ruling of the maghya. 'And if it was not'—i.e., before the speaker's speech it was not a ghayah in reality, but due to the speaker's speech it became a ghayah. Then the beginning of the speech is considered: if the beginning of the speech does not include it, then likewise the ghayah will not enter under the maghya; so it is for extending the ruling—this is an intervening sentence between the condition and the result. The meaning is that this ghayah only serves to extend the ruling, i.e., to draw the ruling up to the ghayah, and the ghayah remains outside the ruling, like 'complete the fast until night'—so the word 'sawm' does not include the night, therefore the ruling does not enter it, making continuous fasting forbidden. And if the beginning of the speech includes the ghayah, like in 'wash your hands up to the elbows', the word 'hand' includes the elbow because its application extends from the fingertips to the shoulders and armpits—then the mention of the ghayah is for dropping what is beyond it. The phrase 'to the elbows' drops the ruling of washing from the shoulders and armpits down to the elbows, and the elbows remain under the ruling of 'wash your hands', so they enter under the maghya, i.e., the ruling of washing. And grammarians have four schools regarding 'ila'. He has detailed what is chosen according to the author. From here he presents evidence for his chosen view. The summary is that grammarians have four schools regarding 'ila'. The first school: inclusion metaphorically—i.e., the ghayah enters under the ruling of the maghya, and this is the real meaning of 'ila', and non-inclusion is metaphorical. The second school: the opposite—the real meaning of 'ila' is that the ghayah does not enter under the ruling of the maghya, and inclusion is metaphorical; so according to this second school, the inclusion of the elbows under the ruling of washing is metaphorical, not real. The third school: literal ambiguity—i.e., 'ila' is shared between both meanings of inclusion and non-inclusion; according to this third school, both inclusion and non-inclusion of the ghayah under the ruling of the maghya are real meanings because in a shared word both meanings are posited. The fourth school: inclusion if what comes after it is of the same genus as what came before; i.e., if what is after 'ila' is of the same genus as what precedes, then what is after enters the ruling of what precedes; if not, then it does not enter. And what we mentioned regarding night—we said that since the beginning of the speech does not include it, the night does not enter under the ruling of the maghya; and regarding the elbows, since the beginning of the speech includes the ghayah, the elbows enter under the ruling of the maghya—this is consistent with the fourth school. The meaning of what we mentioned and what the grammarians mentioned is the same; it is only a difference in expression, because the grammarians said that if the ghayah is of the same genus as the maghya, its meaning is that if the word of the maghya includes the ghayah, this is just a difference in expression and phrasing but unity in meaning. 'We chose this fourth school'—the reason we chose the fourth school is that it is the result of the three schools, and adopting it is virtually acting on the result of the three schools. Because the first two schools are contradictory, so neither side can be certain; they cause doubt. And the third school of ambiguity also causes doubt because in ambiguity no single side can be definitive and certain. Therefore the decision is: if the beginning of the speech does not include the ghayah, then its inclusion under the ruling of the maghya cannot be established due to doubt; if the beginning of the speech includes it, then its exclusion cannot be established due to doubt. In any case, the three schools cause doubt, and the established ruling cannot be changed due to doubt. And some later commentators explained the statements of earlier ones in this manner: 'ila' is for ghayah, and the ghayah does not enter under the maghya absolutely, whether the beginning of the speech includes it or not, whether it is of the same genus as what precedes or not. However, here in 'to the elbows', the ghayah is not for washing; rather it is a ghayah for dropping, so it does not enter under the ruling of dropping, but remains under the ruling of washing. This is because 'yad' is the name of the entire arm from the tips of the fingers to the armpits and shoulders, and this ghayah cannot be the ghayah for washing the whole because washing the whole does not stop at the elbows; rather it goes beyond the elbows to the shoulders and armpits. Thus 'to the elbows' conveys that a part of the whole is being dropped, and it is known that the part whose washing is dropped is that which is connected to the rest, and 'to the elbows' is the ghayah for dropping that part's washing. Therefore the elbows do not enter under the ruling of dropping, but remain under the ruling of washing.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage discusses a key issue in usul al-fiqh: the interpretation of the Arabic particle 'ila' (to/until) in legal texts, specifically whether the endpoint (ghayah) is included in the ruling of the main clause (maghya) or excluded. The author (likely a commentator on Al-Tawdih wa al-Talwih) presents the four well-known madhhabs of grammarians on this issue and then explains why the fourth madhhab is the chosen (mukhtar) position in the Hanafi school.

First, he clarifies two scenarios: when the ghayah is a real endpoint in reality (before the speaker uses 'ila') and when it becomes a ghayah only by the speaker's expression. In both cases, the default is that the ghayah is not included in the ruling of the maghya, as shown by examples like 'I ate the fish up to its head' (the head is not eaten) and 'I sent this garden from this wall to that wall' (the end wall is not included). However, when the word of the maghya naturally includes the ghayah, like 'hand' includes the elbow, then the mention of 'ila' serves to exclude what lies beyond the ghayah, not to exclude the ghayah itself. Thus, in the wudu verse (Quran 5:6), 'wash your hands up to the elbows' means the elbows are included in the washing; only the parts beyond the elbows (the upper arm to shoulders) are excluded.

The four grammatical madhhabs are then detailed: (1) inclusion is the real meaning of 'ila', exclusion is metaphorical; (2) exclusion is real, inclusion is metaphorical; (3) 'ila' is a homonym (mushtarak) with both meanings equally real; (4) inclusion depends on whether the ghayah is of the same genus as the maghya (or, equivalently, whether the maghya term encompasses the ghayah). The author adopts the fourth madhhab because it resolves the doubt created by the contradictory first two and the ambiguous third. He argues that certainty is required for changing the default ruling, and the other three only produce doubt.

Some later commentators offer a twist: in the wudu verse, 'to the elbows' is a ghayah for dropping (isqat), not for washing. Since the hand (yad) includes the whole arm, washing would normally require washing up to the shoulders. The phrase 'to the elbows' thus drops the washing of the upper arm, but the elbows themselves remain subject to washing. This explanation reinforces the fourth madhhab's practical result.

This discussion is critical for Hanafi fiqh because it affects rulings like the obligation to wash the elbows in wudu (Hanafi: elbows are washed) and the prohibition of continuous fasting (not eating after nightfall, since night is not part of the fast day). The Hanafi position consistently follows the principle that certainty is not overturned by doubt, hence the fourth madhhab is preferred.

Key Terms
غاية (ghayah): The endpoint or limit mentioned after the particle 'ila' (to/until).
مغيا (maghya): The main clause or the term being limited by the ghayah; the thing to which the ruling applies up to but potentially including the ghayah.
إلى (ila): The Arabic particle indicating the endpoint of an action or scope; its interpretation in legal texts is subject to dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Quranic verse 'wash your hands up to the elbows' (فاغسلوا أيديكم إلى المرافق), what is the Hanafi ruling regarding the elbows?
A) The elbows are not washed because 'ila' excludes the endpoint.
B) The elbows are washed because 'hand' (يد) linguistically includes the elbows.
C) The elbows are washed only if the intention is to include them.
D) The elbows are not washed because they are of a different genus than the hand.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school adopts the fourth madhhab on 'ila': inclusion of the ghayah depends on whether the maghya term linguistically encompasses it, ensuring legal certainty.

The Fourth Opinion on Ghayah Inclusion: Resolving Doubt through Sadr Kalam
Explaining and justifying the fourth opinion on ghayah inclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the fourth opinion on ghayah inclusion to determine whether a limit enters under the ruling of the mughayya, using the criterion of sadr kalam.

Core Idea
The ghayah is included only if the initial statement covers it; doubt cannot change the ruling.

In usul al-fiqh, one of the classic problems is whether a limit (ghayah) mentioned after a ruling is included under that ruling or not. For example, when Allah says 'wash your hands to the elbows' (ila al-marafiq), are the elbows included? The early opinions (always included, always excluded, or sharing) all lead to doubt. This lesson presents a fourth, synthetic opinion that ties inclusion to whether the initial statement (sadr kalam) conceptually includes the limit. This approach, grounded in the principle that legal rulings cannot be changed on the basis of doubt, offers a clear criterion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Four Opinions
First, recall the three earlier opinions: (1) ghayah is always excluded; (2) ghayah is always included; (3) ghayah is included if it shares the genus of the mughayya, excluded otherwise. The fourth opinion introduces a new criterion: the scope of the sadr kalam.
The fourth opinion is not entirely new but a synthesis from the previous three.
2
Apply the Criterion of Sadr Kalam
Ask: Does the beginning of the statement (e.g., 'wash your hands') semantically include the limit (e.g., 'to the elbows')? If yes, the limit is included; if no, it is excluded. The linguistic analysis determines conceptual coverage.
This is independent of the preposition 'ila' or genus.
3
Test with Examples
Apply to two classic cases: (a) 'fast the day to the night' (ila al-layl) — the initial command to fast the day does not semantically include the night, so the night is excluded. (b) 'wash your hands to the elbows' — the command to wash hands includes the elbows as part of the hands, so they are included.
The same logic applies to other ghayah rulings.
4
Justify the Chosen Opinion
The fourth opinion is preferred because the first three generate doubt (tashkik) — contradictory positions or ambiguous sharing. Since legal rulings are based on certainty (yaqin), doubt cannot override the default. Thus, the clearer criterion of sadr kalam prevails.
This is a rational (aqli) justification, not a text-based one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
میں داخل ہوگا اگر جنس ما قبل سے نہیں ہے تو پھر داخل نہیں ہوگا وما ذکر نانی اللیل ہم نے جولیل کے متعلق کہا کہ صدر کلام چونکہ اس کو متناول نہیں ہے لہذا الیل تحت حکم المغیا داخل نہیں ہوگی اور مرافق میں چونکہ صدر کلام اس غایتہ کو متناول ہے لہذا مرافق تحت حکم المنیا داخل ہوں گی یہ اسی مذہب رابع کے مناسب ہے۔ معنی ما ذکرنا یعنی جو کچھ ہم نے ذکر کیا اور جو کی نحویوں نے ذکر کیا منی متحد ہیں صرف اختلاف عبارت اور اختلاف الفاظ ہے کیونکہ نحویوں نے جو کہا ہے کہ غایتہ اگر جنس معیا سے ہو تو اس کا معنی یہی ہے کہ اگر لفظ مغیا فایتہ کو متناول ہو تو یہ صرف تعبیری اور لفظی فرق اور معنوی اتحاد ہے انما اخترنا هذا المذهب الرابع ہم نے جو مذہب رابع اختیار کیا اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ مذاہب ثلثہ کا یہی نتیجہ ہے اور اسی کا اخذ گویا کہ عمل نتیجة المذاہب الثلثہ ہے کیونکہ مذہبین اولین کو متعار منیں ہیں لہذا کوئی ایک جانب یقینی نہیں ہو سکتی یہ موجب شک ہیں اور مذہب ثالث اشتراک والا یہ بھی موجب شک ہے کیونکہ اشتراک میں کوئی ایک جانب قطعی اور یقینی نہیں ہو سکتی پس فیصلہ یہی ہے که اگر صد سر کلام متناول للعایتہ نہیں تو اس کا دخول تحت حکم المغیا شک کی بنا پر نہیں ہو سکتا اگر صد کام اسے متناول ہے تو اب اس کا خروج شک کے ساتھ نہیں ہوسکتا بہر حال مذاہب ثلثہ موجب شک ہیں اور شک کی بنا پر حکم سابق تبدیل نہیں ہو سکتا۔
It will enter if it is from the same genus as the preceding; if not, it will not enter. And what we mentioned regarding the night: we said that since the beginning of the statement does not include it, therefore the night does not enter under the ruling of the limited, and regarding the elbows, since the beginning of the statement includes that limit, therefore the elbows enter under the ruling of the limited. This is appropriate for this fourth opinion. The meaning of what we mentioned and what the grammarians mentioned are unified; there is only a difference in expression and wording. Because the grammarians said: if the limit is of the same genus as the limited, its meaning is that if the word of the limited includes the limit, then it is only a difference in expression and wording, and a unity of meaning. We chose this fourth opinion because it is the outcome of the three opinions and it is essentially derived from them. Because the first two opinions are contradictory, so neither side can be certain; they are causes of doubt. And the third opinion, which is of sharing, is also a cause of doubt because in sharing no one side can be definitive and certain. Therefore the decision is that if the beginning of the statement does not include the limit, then its entry under the ruling of the limited cannot be based on doubt; if the beginning of the statement includes it, then its exclusion cannot be with doubt. In any case, the three opinions are causes of doubt, and the previous ruling cannot be changed based on doubt.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage presents a fourth opinion (madhhab rabi') regarding whether the limit (ghayat) is included under the ruling of the limited (mughayya) phenomenon. The author explains that this opinion is based on the scope of the initial statement (sadr kalam): if the initial statement does not include the limit, then the limit is not included in the ruling; if it does include it, then it is included. This is illustrated with examples: 'the night' (layl) as a limit where the initial statement does not cover it, so it is excluded, whereas 'the elbows' (marafiq) in the context of wudu are covered by the initial command to wash the hands, so they are included. The author then reconciles this with the grammarians' view that if the limit is of the same genus, it is included; the difference is merely verbal. The justification for choosing this fourth opinion is that the first three opinions (inclusion always, exclusion always, or sharing) all lead to doubt, and legal rulings cannot be changed based on doubt. Thus, the fourth opinion is the most sound synthesis.

Key Terms
غاية (ghāyah): The limit or endpoint mentioned in a legal ruling, e.g., 'to the elbows' (ila al-marafiq).
مغيا (mughayyā): The phenomenon that is limited by the ghayah; what comes before the limit.
صدر كلام (ṣadr kalām): The beginning or initial part of a statement that indicates the general ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Whether the ghayah is of the same genus as the mughayya
B) Whether the beginning of the statement (sadr kalam) includes the ghayah
C) Whether the ghayah is mentioned with the particle 'ila'
D) The intention of the speaker

Key Takeaway
The ghayah is included only if the initial statement (sadr kalam) semantically includes it; this avoids doubt and respects the principle that certainty cannot be overridden by uncertainty.

Inclusion of the Ending Limit (Ghayat) in Iqrar and Khiyar: A Hanafi Usul Dispute
Presenting the disputes among Hanafi imams on the inclusion of ghayat in iqrar and khiyar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, the Sahibayn, and Zufar on the inclusion of the ending limit in declarations from 'min ... ila', and apply the same principle to khiyar al-shart.

Core Idea
The Hanafi imams differ on whether the limit after 'ila' is included — a linguistic nuance with concrete legal consequences.

This lesson examines a classic usul dispute among the Hanafi masters concerning the interpretation of phrases like 'from one to ten.' The particle 'ila' marks an endpoint, but is that endpoint included or excluded? The answer affects the amount owed in a debt declaration and the period of an option in a sale. We will trace the reasoning of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic structure
Recognize that 'min' indicates the starting point and 'ila' indicates the ending limit. The question is whether the object of 'ila' is included in the range or excluded.
In Arabic, 'min...ila...' sets a range; the legal effect depends on whether the limit is part of what is included.
2
State Abu Hanifa's view on iqrar
Abu Hanifa rules that the ending limit after 'ila' is not included. Therefore, in 'from one to ten,' the debt covers one through nine, excluding ten. Reason: 'ila' indicates exclusion of its object.
3
State the Sahibayn's view on iqrar
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule that both the beginning and ending limits are included. Thus, the debt covers one through ten. Reason: the number ten cannot exist without its parts.
4
State Zufar's view on iqrar
Zufar rules that neither limit is included, so the debt covers two through nine, omitting both one and ten. Reason: both 'min' and 'ila' serve as exclusions.
5
Apply to khiyar al-shart
In a sale where the seller says 'I have the option until tomorrow,' Abu Hanifa includes tomorrow within the option period. The absolute phrase 'he has the option' is limited by 'until tomorrow,' which only excludes periods after tomorrow.
Here, the ending limit 'tomorrow' is included in the option, consistent with Abu Hanifa's general approach of excluding the limit only when it is the object of omission.

نان قال له على من درهم الى عشرة الخ اس کلام میں لفظ من ابتداء غانیہ کے لئے ہے اورالی انتہائے غایہ کے لئے یدخل الاول یہ کی ابتدائے غایہ تو ضرور داخل ہے کیونکہ یہ اپنے مافوق کیلئے جز ہے اور وجود کل بدون الجز محال ہے لا الآخر اور آخر غایتہ جو بعد از الی ہے ۔ ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک داخل نہیں ہوگی لہذا اس اقرار کے ساتھ يجب تسعه نو دراهم واجب ہوں گے وعندهما اور صاحبین کے نزدیک رونوں غایتین داخل ہوں گی فتجب عشره عندهما کیونکه عشره بغیر عشره اجزاء یعنی عشره و حدات کے بدوں موجود نہیں ہو سکتا لہذا غایتین داخل ہوں گی. وعند زفر اور امام زفر کے نزدیک دونوں خواتین داخل نہیں ہوں گی فیجب ثمانية عنده وتدخل الغاية في الخيار عندہ اور امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک غایتہ خیارہ میں داخل رہے گی مثلا ایک شخص بیع کرتا ہے اور کہتا ہے انہای البائع بالخيار الی غیر تو غد خیار میں داخل ہو جائیگی تو خیار اس کے لئے عد میں ثابت رہیگا لان قوله على انه بالخيار کیونکہ علی انسه بالخیار کا لفظ کسی یوم کے ساتھ مقید نہیں ہے اپنے مفہوم کے لحاظ سے مافوق الغد کو شامل ہے تو لفظ الى الغد اسقاط کے لئے ہے جو ماورائے عد کو ساقط عن الخیار کر رہا ہے لہذا خد داخل في السقوط نہیں ہو گی اس کا ماوراء ساقط ہو جائیگا اور غد داخل في الخيار رہیگی ۔
If he said: 'I have a claim upon him from one dirham to ten' – in this speech, the word 'min' is for the beginning limit, and 'ila' is for the ending limit. The first, which is the beginning limit, necessarily enters because it is a part of what is above it, and the existence of the whole without the part is impossible. The last, which is the ending limit after 'ila', according to Abu Hanifa does not enter, so with this declaration nine dirhams are owed. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), both limits enter, so ten dirhams are owed, because ten cannot exist without ten parts, i.e., ten units, so both limits enter. According to Zufar, both limits do not enter, so eight dirhams are owed. And according to Abu Hanifa, the limit enters in the option (khiyar). For example, a person makes a sale and says: 'he – the seller – has the option until tomorrow' – then 'tomorrow' enters into the option, so the option will be established for him for tomorrow. Because his statement 'he has the option' is not restricted to any day; its meaning include beyond tomorrow. So the phrase 'until tomorrow' is for omission, which removes beyond tomorrow from the option. Therefore, 'tomorrow' is not included in the omission; its beyond is omitted, and 'tomorrow' remains within the option.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage presents a classic usul dispute regarding the inclusion of the limit in legal statements. In the example of an iqrar (declaration) 'from one dirham to ten', the particle 'min' indicates the starting point, which is included because the whole cannot exist without the starting unit. The dispute centers on whether the ending limit (ten) is included. Abu Hanifa says it is not included, so the debtor owes nine dirhams (from 1 up to but not including 10). The two companions (Sahibayn) say it is included, so ten dirhams are owed. Zufar says neither the beginning nor the ending limit is included, so eight dirhams are owed (from 2 to 9). The second application is to khiyar (option) in a sale: if the seller says 'I have the option until tomorrow', Abu Hanifa rules that tomorrow is included within the option period. The reasoning: the statement 'he has the option' is absolute and would extend indefinitely; 'until tomorrow' limits it by omitting the period after tomorrow. Since the limit 'tomorrow' is the point of omission itself, it is not omitted, so the option remains valid for tomorrow.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrār): A legal declaration or admission, especially of a debt.
خيار (khiyār): An option or right to rescind a contract within a specified period.
غاية (ghāyah): End limit or goal; in this context, the endpoint of a range or period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the iqrar example 'from one dirham to ten', how many dirhams are owed according to Abu Hanifa if the ending limit is not included?
A) Eight
B) Nine
C) Ten
D) One

Key Takeaway
The inclusion of the ghayat (limit) after 'ila' is a matter of usul dispute among the Hanafi imams, with practical consequences for debt amounts and option periods.
